Few royal careers in modern times have
been marked with sharper contrasts than
that' of the Empress Frederick. Born
Princess Royal and heir apparent to the
British throne, she became successively
the Crown Princess of Prussia, the con
sort'of the ruler of Germany, then in a
few months wholly bereft of power, re
taining but the empty title of the widow
of an Emperor. Following the period of
mourning for her imperial husband. Came
the harsh, unfilial conduct of her son, who
did his utmost to ; make her feel that in
Germany there was no abiding place for
her. During the ensuing years she had
been practically lost to the public eye, un
til last spring, when it was reported that
she had secretly married Count Saecktorf.
This report, however, proved to he un
true.

oil transportation was at that time, in the
hands of parties who were in the oil busi
ness, operating from San Pedro; that
when oil was discovered in Southern Cali
fornia the people called upon the railroad
to assist them. Their only avenue of ex-,
porting their oil was by water. They were
compelled to put their oil in barrels or
iron drums or sell !t to companies.
"Thereby," sa?d Mr. Sproule, "the in
dustry was troubled, and the producers
called upon us for help. Prior to that time
we had a rate of 1 2-10 mills per gallon
from Newhall to San Francisco to try to
capture the oil from the water carriers
via. Ventura. We, however, made a condi
tion^ that the exporter furnish the cars.
When the people of Los Angeles called
upon us for the same rate they had no
cars, and it was considerable expense to
get them. We saw an opportunity of de
veloping a new industry. But for our ac
tion in the matter people would have been
compelled to "sell to private oil companies
or charter craft for the purpose of trans
porting oil. They would have created
new lines of transportation. It was inter
est in the development of a new industry

LONDON, Aug. 5.â The news of the
death of the Dowager Empress Frederick
spread slowly in London. The Associated
Press gave Buckingham Palace, Marlbor
ough House and the Mansion House the
first information that her Majesty had
passed away. As all the members of the
royal family and many of the officials are
out of town on holidays, it is not expected
that official recognition of her' Majesty's
death will be issued before morning. The
bells of St. Paul's Cathedral will be tolled
when the Home Secretary. Mr. Ritchie,
officially informs the Lord Mayor/ of the
death of the Dowager Empress. The offi
cials of the German Embassy had not
officially heard the news at 7:30 p. m.
Their, colleagues at The Hague, on the
other hand, received the news of the.de
mise of the Dowager Empress before it
had occurred, ! and halfmasted the lega
tion flag at about 3 p. m. The Foreign
Minister and the members of the legation
here cabled the legation at T*he Hague
when the mistake was discovered and the
halfmasted flag was hastily removed.
London Bells to Be Tolled.

hand. The Americans here are seldom

diately halfmasted. King Edward and
Queen Alexandra will leave here for Marl

likely they will start for Berlin until

Proposed Electric Line Changes.
SAN JOSE, Aug. 5.â There are some
contemplated changes in the management
of the San Jose and Santa Clara Elec
tric Railway to take place at the annual
meeting of that corporation Wednesday
The road was recently purchased by
George and Hugh Center, and it is said
Hugh Center will succeed J. P. Burke
as manager of the road. The Centers
own the Alum Rock road, and a new
corporation will be formed into which
both roads. will be merged. Plans are
also to be considered for transforming
the Alum Rock line from a motor into
an electric railway. The reSuction of the
fare from Santa Clara to San Jose from
10 cents to 5 cents will also be consid
ered.

REDLANDS, Aug. 5.--A cloudburst in
the Santa Ana Canyon, the principal feed
er of the San Gabriel Valley water shed,
sent a volume of. water down this' after
noon .which-: destroyed ranch, 'mill and
power property to the extent of $200,000.
The storm was entirely unexpected/ not
a cloud, being In the sky two hours before
the precipitation. The water washed out
mountain roads, telephone and telegraph
lines and entirely . destroyed communica
tion, with the farther mountain resorts. .-
Several hunting . parties = were out after
deer, and up to 9 o'clock to-night were
still missing, but since they were hunting
on the hills it is thought they escaped the
first rush of water that flooded the lower
Cienegas ] and are prevented " from return
ing, to camp by the torrents still pouring
down the bottoms of the canyons. ;

Attacks were made on the cars on the
night of June 18 and some of them were
burned. A large body of police succeeded
in dispersing- the crowd, but on Sunday
the disturbances were renewed and on
Monday six cars were burned in the Lar
geo De Sao Francisco, which was finally
cleared by the police after several per
sons had been wounded. The following
day barricades were built In the Quividot
and other important business streets In the
vicinity of the Largeo de Sao Francisco,
and the thoroughfares were kept cleared
only by the repeated charges of boththo
cavalry and Infantry- Quiet Â¦was restored
on Wednesday night by an announcement
that the company would return to the old
schedule of fares. The police aroused al
most a universal protest . by their reck
lessness and severity, but the only demon
strations against them were entirely un
organized and almost puerile. They were
apparently directed,' Bryan says, against
the police as individuals rather than as
Government authorities. The only legal
questions as yet growing out of the dis
turbances have been suits against indi
vidual policemen for undue violence.

N. W., WASHINGTON," Aug. 5.â Rio Ja
neiro was the scene of seTious rioting
June 18, according to an official report re
cived by the State Department from
Charles Page Bryan, Minister to Brazil.
Five persons were killed and 200 were
wounded before quiet was restored. Bryan
says the disturbance was not of a political
character and no traces of a revolution
ary attempt against the Government could
be discovered. The rioting lasted several
days. It began in consequence of an in
crease in the fares made by the Sao Chris
tavos Street Railway Company under the
terms of a recent contract with the Gov
ernment.

Linn & HofTs fishing schooners came to

camp, where they found two of them dead
and evidences . of another having left.

gold watch. They said they were going"
back to bury the men. This was the last
seen of them by Scott.

SMALL BOY SETS
FIRE TO A HALL
Kansas City Exposition
? -Building Burns to
the Ground.
Blaze Starts in Rubbish and
Quickly Gets Beyond
Control.
KANSAS dry, Aug. 5.â The exposition
building erected during the boom of 1887
at a -cost of over $200,000 was destroyed
by fire in less than two hours' time this
afternoon. The fire started shortly after
1 o'clock within ten feet of where Patrick
Gilmore stood when he directed his
famous band at the exercises dedicating
the building fourteen years ago.* A boy'
among a crowd which had gathered to
watch a large circus that had pitched its
tents across the street set fire, in a spirit
of mischief, to\some loose rubbish lying
on the floor. In a moment the flame
leaped beyond control, spreading almost
instantly throughout the whole building,
which occupied a half-block square. No
effort was made to save the building.
The exposition building was situated at
Twelfth street and Kansas avenue, three
miles from the center of the city. It had
not been occupied for ten years. Recently
a syndicate planned to raze it to make
way for modern buildings. The loss 13
nominal.
Rifled Trunks Found Near Graves.
WICHITA. Kans.. Aug. 5.â A dispatch
to the Eagle from Marlow. I. T., says that
two newly made graves have been found
in the Klowa, Comanche and Apache
country, fifteen miles west of Duncan,
and near them some rifled trunks and
boxes. Deputy United States Marshal
Thomas has left Fort Sill for the acena
to make an investigation.

TRTjCKEE. Aug. 5. â Joe Lewis, aged 16^
years, and Earl McGlashan, aged 13. haX
an excitlns experience with a family of
bears this evening near Deer Creek, eight
miles from Truekee. The boj'swere hunt-
Ing deer and -were following a wounded
buck when they suddenly came upon a
bear and three cubs. They fired one shot
at the old bear at a distance of less than
forty yards, and -wounded her so severely
that she forsook her cubs and took to the
brush. Each boy killed a cub, and to
gether they -wounded the other cub so
badly that it fell down several times
Â¦while following the mother into the
brush. Uarkness coming on the boys car
ried the dead cubs Into town, and will re
turn in the morning to look for the
wounded bears. The cubs weigh forty
pounds each.

Union Adopts Resolution Declaring
Against TTse of I*iquor.
At a largely attended meeting the Pile
Drivers' and Bridge Builders' Union "held
yesterday afternoon a resolution was
unanimously adopted declaring that the
sense of the union was for total absti
nence during the continuance of the
strike. The passage of the resolution was
greeted with hearty cheers.
The men realize that the strike Is likely
to" prove a prolonged affair and that to
win it they must husband their resources.
Leaders of the movement at the meeting
yesterday spoke of the benefits that would
accrue "to the' members of the union
through their evincing, a desire to shun
the saloons, not only in a monetary sense
but through the fact that by shunning
liquor they would remove themselves from
all danger of hazarding the success of
the strike by the violence which intoxi
cated men are likely, to indulge In. Ef
forts will be made to secure co-operation
of the other unions in the movement. /

Issues a Circular.
Beer-Bottlers' ' Protective Association
The Pacific Coast Beer-Bottlers' Pro
tective Association has prepared a circu
lar for distribution , among patrons,' an
nouncing that the association will protect
John Rapp &. Son against the boycott by
keeplng hands oft the trade of that lirm.
The circular continues:
- Our action in this matter may not meet
with the approval of all, but thooewho are
in a position to know the facts, certainly would
be with us if properly informed. We have been
refused the privilege of hiring our own men.
Our members have been compelled under threat
of ' strike and boycott to reinstate discharged
employes. I without . the privilege of arbitration,
upon which clause the union makes its great
boast. In spite of the. fact that the union had
an agreement with us for one year regulating
hours and wages, they disregarded this agree
ment and made new demands of Increased
wages and shorter hours before the agreement
then in force had expired, to which demands
we submitted, r 'Â¦"-.' " '.."Â¦Â¦Â¦ :: Â¦Â¦>Â¦
We have been told not to give our horses
to a certain blacksmith to shoe, and In all of
these instances we have submitted to the de
mands of the union in order 'to avoid com
plications. New difficulties arose from . day
to day. and in all instances arbitration was
refused. Our employes have received all that
they asked for. in wages and hours without
opposition,- but .the dictation from/ outside
sources became unbearable, and . the last
straw came when," without a. moment's no
tice, the men were: called out of -one of-our
member's establishments because said mem
ber received goods on a .truck with police pro
tection;' â¢ ; . Â¦
Will Stand Firm, for Increase From
S3 to $3 50 a Bay.
OAKLAND, Aug. 5.â The striking
painters of Oakland did not secure their
increase in wages and there are Indica
tions of a prolonged struggle. The master
painters to-night refused to grant the
increase of wages demanded and the men
who struck this morning have . decided
that they v.-ill stay out until their" union
has an opportunity to meet and consider
the situation.
The painters submitted to the employers
a wage scale which fixed the minimum
day's waeres at $3 50 instead of $3, as at
present. The employers refused the In
crease and .this morning 300 painters,
paper hangers and decorators left their
work. As the demand for the Increase in
wages was not a new one the employers
were. In a large measure, prepared for the
strike. Contracts ofÂ»large size and with
time limitations were all refused. The
employers waited to see whether the men
would make good their threat of walking
out, and when 'they did it was thought
that the result in Oakland would go with
the result of the same demand in San
Francisco.'
The Master Painters' Association held a
meeting to consider the situation and
finally decided to stand by their original
refusal, and they empowered the execu
tive committee to prepare an .announce
ment for the press, which was given out
as follows:
OAKLAND. Aug. B. 1901.
The members of the Master Painters' Asso
ciation of Alameda County, at a meeting held
this evening unanimously indorsed the action
taken. at a former meeting not to grant the
demand of the painters' union for an increase
of wages to the minimum of {3 50 per day.
L. ,X. COBBLEDICK, Secretary.
The members of the executive commit
tee are: President.' James Cahlll: secre
tary, L.. N. .Cobbledick; E. C. Thurber,
John Donovan and C. L. Metzger. There
were present about forty of the members
of the association. .
In speaking of the stand taken by the
master painters. Secretary Cobbledick
said: "We have decided to stand to
gether as one man. We are unanimous
in the stand taken and will not grant the
demands of the journeymen painters un
der- any 'circumstances'. Non-union men
are now . applying for work, and in a
week's time we expect to have all of the
places vacated by the union men filled.
The San Francisco association Is standing
with us in the matter and we have no
fear of the outcome. This strike is -in
augurated principally by the poorer Â¦work
men, .who were not being paid $3 50 a day
The result is that the good workmen have
been drawn into the strike to assist the
others." . - â¢
President : R. P. Poorman of the Jour
neymen Painters'. Union said: "We met
this evening and indorsed the stand we
took two months ago. "We will not recede
from our demands for a wage of $3 50 a
day. "We will meet again Thursday even
ing and may . take some further action
in the matter, but I- am not prepared to
say what that action will be." â¢
OAKLAND PAINTERS OUT.
MATOR PHELAN failed to get a
conference of the board of di
rectors of the Employers' Asso
ciation yesterday. M. F. Mich
ael, attorney for the associa
tion, had gone out of the city, and re
sponses were not obtained to requests
made to Individual directors that they be
present.
At 11:30 a. m. the Mayor met a number
of merchants in his office, among whom
were Raphael Welll and A. M. Davis. All
but one of them disavowed any connec
tion with the Employer!*' Association.
These men decided against asking for an
other meeting of the Employers' Associa
tion, and at 3 o'clock Mayor Phelan sub
mlÂ£ted Attorney Michael's letter of August
2 to the labor leaders.
The Mayor gave out the following state
ment late In the afternoon:
The situation is as follows: The City Front
Federation, of which the Teamsters' Brother
hood Is a part, proposed the following basis
for the re-establishment of peace:
City Front Federation,
Southwest corner East and Mission Streets.
SAX FRANCISCO. Cal., July 31, 190L ,--
Hon. James D. Phelan, Mayor, San Fran
cisco Cal. â Sir: As per your request, the
City Front Federation hereby submits the
following propositions as a basis for the
re-establishment of peace: The Employ
ers' agrees that its members
â¢will not discriminate against any employer Â¦
or labor because such employer chooses to ,
employ union men only.
' The Employers' Association agrees-that
its members will not discriminate against
any employe because he chooses, for reasons
sufficient to himself, to be a member of a
labor organization.
The Employers' Association agrees that
all employes who are locked . out or who
have quit work on account of the exist
ing difficulty shall be reinstated. Labor
organizations agree that any member who
is employed shall, while in such employ,
obey any orders given . to him concerning
the work to be performed." Whenever any
difficulties arise between employers and em
ployes no strike or lockout shall be insti
tuted until the parties in interest shall
have done all in their power to adjust such
difficulties.
JOHN KEAN, Acting. President.
â¢ By order of the City Front Federation.
THOMAS WESTOHY, Rec. Secretary.
Adopted July 30, 1901.

SAN FRANCISCO, Aug. 2, 190L

The Employers' Association Is willing to
recommend to the members of the Dray
men's Association that they fill all present

work. Irrespective of .whether the applicant
belongs to a union or not, upon the follow
ing terms:
First â That the employe will obey all law
ful orders of his employer.
Secondâ That the employe will not, direct

low employe, against his free will, to join

to employ none but union men. .Â¦Â¦â¢Â¦â¢
Thirdâ That the employe will not, directly
or indirectly, engage In or support any sym
pathetic strike or boycott. _ *
I am Instructed to define the attitude of
the Employers' Association with regard to'
â¢ unionism, as follows: .
First â The association recognizes the right
of labor to organize to ameliorate Its con
dition.
Second â The association has not In the
matter of employment discriminated in
favor of or against union men, and has no
intention of doing so.
Thirdâ With regard to the adjustment of
differences between employer and employe,'
this association has made It clear in Its
previous correspondence with you that the

left to the employer and own employes,
' without Interference from the officers or

Fourth â It has been -the practice of this

Labor Leaders' Objection. .
The representatives of the Federation took
exception to several points and on. Saturday I
asked the Board of Directors of the Employers'
Association to consider the objections, inas
much as the letter h*ad come from their execu
tive committee, with a view of modifying or
explaining some of the terms. . I made an
explanation, and while the board of directors
refused to modify the language they explained
its meaning. There was no serious objection to
the first and second conditions of re-employ
ment; but the third was objected to by the
representatives of the federation on the ground
that it was a- legal right of the employe to
engage In a sympathetic strike or boycott. The
employers answered that If It is a legal right
it can be exercised, and they bo desired me to
explain to the labor leaders. â¢
The Employers' Association disavow any
intention td sympathetically boycott any
employer of union labor by refusing to sell him
goods. This they state in their fourth para
graph defining the attitude of their association
with regard to unionism and is a concession
to the federat.on. The employers also yield
their original Intention of employing none but
non-union men and concede the right of their
men to form organizations.
I delivered the letter 'to-day to Messrs. Furu-
Sfcth and Casey, at a conference at which City
Attorney Lane and P. II. McCarthy, president
of the Building Trades Council, were present,
explaining these several matters -as I received
them from the Employers' Association, and
they were to report the letter to their,feder
ation this evcnlns.
End of Negotiations.
This morning I conferred with-, representa
tives of business Interests and explained the
situation. There were present gentlemen repre
senting the shipping Interests, the commercial
Interests and the retail trade,- and the Munici
pal League, as well as a representative of the
Employers' Association, who was not present,
however,' in that capacity. The. only point
argued was the â¢ advisability of - asking the
Kmployers' Association to meet again to strike
out their third condition of re-employment,
namely, that the employe will not engage in a
sympathetic strike or boycott, on the ground
that It wa3 a legal I right of the employe and
that such a modification might serve to pro
mote peace; but as the employers had agreed
not to sympathetically boycott employers oC
union labor by refusing to sell them goods, it
waa considered 1 , that that was an offset and that
such an agreement at any rate had no legal
force or effect. .. There was, nothing that could

. '-", JAMES D. PHELAN.
THE number of men working* on
the water front since the com
mencement of the strike was
doubled yesterday, but the same
congestion of traffic still exists.
On every. wharf along the*front there was
a gang of men at work, and the large
number of Idle men in evidence on every
block of East street was the only visible
evidence of the mighty struggle between
labor and capital that is in progress in
this city. '
Among the men who are able to Judge
of the progress made by crews in handling
cargoes, however, there is a feeling -that
the strikers have succeeded in making
great strides toward a general tie-up of
the shipping. Vessels that were scheduled
to leave days ago succeed In getting out
of the harbor, but they leave several days
behind time and with their* holds only half
filled.
Vessels that entered the harbor loaded
to the gunwales with freight and that
were due to leave port within two or
three days after their arrival have been
unable to get away,, owing to the fact
that, though plenty of men are obtainable
to handle freight, these men cannot dis
charge or load a vessel in the time al
lowed them.
With freight coming from the holds of
vessels and load after load arriving hour
ly from the wholesale houses, the ship
owners are greatly handicapped, but they
have succeeded in accomplishing a won
derful lot of work. At the wharves of the
various steamship companies "bunk
ships," or floating boarding houses, have
been established, where non-union bands
are boarded and lodged. The first few
days of the strike the men who took the
places of the union men were allowed to
board and room where they pleased, but
the strikers' pickets were so vigilant that
they stoppec^the men on their way to and
from work and won many of them from
their employers. The number of men who
reported for work every morning- was be
ing gradually reduced, until the. shipping
men gained a knowledge of the tactics
being pursued by the strikers. Since the
establishment of the floating boarding
houses on the front there has been little
accomplished by the strikers in securing
recruits.
Floating Boarding-Houses.
The steamer Colon is being used by the
Pacific Mail Steamship Company as a
boarding and lodging place for the hands
employed on the Mail dock. The company
has 250 men quartered on the vessel. Ar
rangements have been made whereby the
wives, sons or daughters of the men who
are employed can draw their wages, thu3
relieving them of any need they, might
have to leave the scene_of their labors.
The firms of Ben..ett & Goodall, Eschen
& Minor and Thomas Young & Co. have
chartered the Ohio and will quarter all
the men they hire to take the places of
the strikers on the vessel. Cooks and wait
ers have been hired and arrangements
have been completed to furnish accommo
dations for more than 500 men. The Ohio
will be anchored In Mission Bay and the
men will be taken back and forth in tugs
to the wharves where they are to work.
In consequence of this move on the part
of the ship owners and boss freight hand
lers the force of pickets employed by the
unions has been greatly increased. Mem
bers of the unions patrol the bay in all
sorts of craft and. they lose no opportunity
to approach the vessels on which the men
are living and make every endeavor to
win them over to the cause of unionism.
The work of discharging the cargo of
the collier Tellus was commenced yester
day shortly after noon at Folsom-street
dock No. 2, where the Dunsmulr bunkers
are located. A gang of twelve negroes at
tempted the task of emptying the im
mense holds of the vessel, but as they
were all green hands they made slow
progress.
The experienced longshoremen work in
the holds of a vessel totally unmindful
of the Iron buckets, each containing a ton
day by the river steamer/ Sonoma. The
fruit was reshiDped in cars from Adams
wharves.
There has been no disturbance, either
on the water front or among the team
sters. The few non-union drivers who are
working are unguarded, and there Â¦ has
been no attempt to" molest them. The
police report no trouble on. the harbor,
the sailors' union delegates making no ef
fort to resist the guard that has been
placed on the Adams docks. The situation
on this side of the bay is controlled
by the movement cf affairs in San Fran
cisco, and the unions interested look to\
the headquarters across the bay for direc
tion concerning their future action..
There were a number of patients treated
at the Harbor Emergency Hospital yes
terday, but there were no serious cases,
most of the injured being non-union
stevedores hurt through their lack of
knowledge of the way to handle freight.
The force of clerks employed by A. M.
Simpson were on hand bright and early
yesterday morning to unload the steam'
schooner Signal. The schooner Gotama
was pulled alongside the lumber vessel
and arrangements were made to transfar
the cargo to her. .The first sling of timbers
had hardly been passed over the sMe.
however, when the captain and' mate of
the Gotama put on their coats and re
fused to handle any more of the cargo.
At the transport dock the loading of th-e
Meade continues. The Government offl
clals had considerable doubt as to their
ability to get the transport ready for sea
by the loth and had made arrangements
to replace her for the trip with the War
ren, which is expected in dally. So much
work was accomplished yesterday, how
ever, that the latter plan was abandoned.
The arrivals yesterday were th* City of
Puebla. the Milton and the Coronado.
The crew of each of these vessels quit
as soon as the ships reached the wharves.
The firemen on the City of Puebla did cot
go out as the order issued by the City
Front Federation does not affect the ves
sels of the Pacific Coast Steamship Com
pany.
The Pomona, which was scheduled to
sail for Eureka yesterday at 2 p. m., did
not get away until after 5. She was not
fully loaded when she pulled away - from
the dock. She was the only vessel to clear
during the day.
Pomona Alone Gets to Sea.
There was more fruit landed at the
Jackson street wharf yesterday than was
landed on any two days since the incep
tion of the strike. There seemed to be
little difficulty encountered In securing
teams to haul it to the commission houses
and consumers.
At the Oceanic dock there are fifty men
employed on the steamships Australia and
Mariposa. The Australia will be ready
to sail on Wednesday and it i3 expected
that the Mariposa will be ready for sea
when her sailing date arrives.
The collier Titania which arrived yes
terday will be moved to Rosenf eld's coal
bunkers at the foot of Mission street this
morning, where the work -of discharging
her cargo will be commenced. The col
lier Milton is anchored in the stream tnd
men are engaged in transferring her cargo
into the'hulk Germania. The coal on the
Bristol is being taken out of the vessel
and put in the bunkers at the sugar re
finery. . '
The work of coaling the Chilean mail
steamer Aconcagua is progressing rapidly
at the Oakland wharf. She is also bein?
loaded with wheat at the same time. She
will probably be brought to this side of
the bay this afternoon. She will dock at
Howard street pier No. 3, to which
freight for her is being hauled.
At the dock of the Oregon Railway and
Navigation Company the steamers Colum
bia and George W. Klder are being load
ed. It is expected that the first name.1
vessel will be ready to sail to-day. The
100 men who are handling the freight
destined for these vessels are living and
sleeping on the dock and extreme pre
cautions are being taken to prevent the
strikers or their sympathizers from ap
proaching them.

PAINTERS LEAVE WORK.
Andrew Furuseth, secretary of the Sail
ore' Union of the Pacific, said last night
that for the present at least the strike
â¢would not be extended beyond San Fran
cisco and San Francisco Bay.
"While we are conducting a merely local
fight," Mr. Furuseth said, "we do not in
tend to extend it to other ports. That
will only be done when necessity compels
it. For the present no reason exists for
such a step. The fight is on in real
earnest. We have gone into this fight
to win, and we shall win it. too. So far
as the City Front Federation is con
cerned the unions affiliated therewith are
in a position to stand a siege of two
months without serious difficulty. We
were hoping a prolongation of the strike
might have been averted, but through the
obstinacy of the Employers' Association,
which is determined to do all in its power
to kill the labor organizations in this city,
business will get another setback far
more serious than may at this time be
imagined, i ?Ye ask but for little, but that
little, affecting as it does the principles
of organized labor, was denied us. We did
not ask anything- the employers might not
have freely granted without loss of dig
nity, but our r<?quâ¬st was met with scorn.

SAILOES TO TAKE ACTION.
"One of the reasons why we shall not*
grant this demand is the increase in the
cost of material. Oils and leads are high
er-now than they have been for some time
and to pay our men more than they are
receiving now would mean ruin to our
business. We are backed in our determin
ation by the Master Painters' Association Â¦
of Oakland."
"We have decided that the condition of
our business does not warrant the pay
ment of higher wages to brush hands. We
pay the men what they are worth, and
every member of the association has men
in his employ to whom he pays more than
$3 00 per day. Evidently the order issued
by the union does not affect these men,
for they are still at work.
E. H. Black, president of the Master
Painters' Association, said last night:
"We were informed three months ago
that an increase of the pay of painters
would be demanded by the union August
5. We appointed a committee to meet with
a committee from the painters, but the
meeting resulted unsatisfactorily. The
members of the association have met
often since then and thoroughly can
vassed the situation. As a result of our
deliberations we have determined to re
fuse to grant the demand. Our members
are under ?500 bonds to live up to the laws
of the association and so far only one
member has shown a- disposition to grant
tire increase.
Cannot Afford to Grant Demand.
Xinety-four shops have been reported
to the council as in accord with the above
mentioned request. Many shops which
are supposed to be definitely opposed to
the raise have signified their willingness
to employ men at ?3 50 per day. The
Building Trades Council, therefore, feels
confident that it is only a matter of time
until the other employing painters will
see the wisdom of doing likewise. The
council desires to state to the public that
as far as the painting industry is con
cerned, building construction in the city
will not be retarded. The Building Trades
Council desires also to announce to the
public that the Building Trades Coun
cil is the only order concerned in the
controversy and. that being true, the
public need not fear that any erratic or
overt action will be committed, but, on
the contrary, the council will conduct the
matter in its usual conservative manner,
looking only to the best interest of the
city and State.
"Reports from the pickets which were
appointed to the different shops all point
to a grand success, as far as the $3 50 per
day is concerned. Many shops which"
were considered doubtful up to this morn
ing have conceded the raise in pay and
stated that the demand made by the
Building Trades Council on the 4th of
last May in behalf of Painters' Union No.
73 was a very just one.
If the employers are organized and
united to resist the demands of their men,
the Building Trades Council, which is
handling the flgrht for the painters. Is
equally well prepared to fight the matter
to the end. At the meeting of Painters'

that a great number of the important
shops bed already conceded the increase
in salary. The painters look upon the flght
as already virtually won and confident
ly assert that almost all the employers
will give In this morning.

Both Sides Are Prepared. '
In accordance with their preconcerted
plan, the members of Painters' Union No.
73 reported for work as usual yesterday
morning, but on the refusal of their em
ployers to grant their demand for an in
crease of 50 cents per day In their pay
they walked out. The demand for a raise
in wages had been made three months
ago. so both the men and the employers
were fully prepared for'the action which
was taken yesterday morning. In fact,
the employers have formed an association
for the express purpose of resisting the
demands of their men. The Building
Trades Council has taken the fight in
hand and will have the entire manage
ment of the controversy, in bo far as the
painters' . end of it is concerned. The ac
tion which both bodies have taken thus
far seems to indicate that the struggle will
not be of short duration.
The master painters appear to be
strongly organized and claim that almost
every employing painter of importance in
the city is a member of their association.
The rules of the association place a line
of jaOO on any member who shall increase
the salary of his men to $3 50 per day.
The master painteis claim that there are
between 500 and 600 painters out of em
ployment because of the strike and that
J. H. Keefe is the only boss painter of
importance who has granted the demands
of the union.

Five union minersâ Jacob > Williamson,
John Erickson, Nat Blehlen, Mike John
ston and John Mynetti â secured search
warrants from Judge Cabaniss yesterday
afternoon to recover possession of their
blankets and personal effects, which, they
said, were being illegally detained in the
office of the Draymen's Association at 109
California street. They said they had
been engaged by an employment agent at
Los Angeles to come here to work. Noth
ing was said about the nature of the
work, aiTd when they arrived here they
were taken to the office of the Draymen's
Association, where they deposited their
blankets and other personal effects. They
are all Russian Finns and can speak very
little English. When they learned that
they were to be asked to drive teams be
cause union teamsters were out on strike
they refused to work, as they are union
miners. They were then asked to work
as longshoremen, but again refused, and
were, they said, ordered out of the office
and their blankets and other effects which
they demanded were not returned.

Shipping Intelligence.
ARRIVED.
Coal Cargoes Arriving.
More than 25,000 tons of coal arrived In
port during the last thirty-six hours and
most of it is finding its way into circula
tion. The Titanlabrought in 5558 tons, the
Milton 4648 tons, the Matteawan 5000 tona,
Tellus 3500 tons, Washtenaw 4000 tons and
the British ship Dumferline 3000 tons from
Newcastle, Australia. All of these vessels
will be discharged before the week Is out,
so there is no fear of a coal famine for a
few days to come.
Has a Japanese Crew.
The steamship South Portland has a
Japanese crew. While the vessel was on
the Sound Captain Hall had a dispute
with some of his men, so he discharged
them all and secured Japanese sailors and
firemen. The vessel was expected yester
day morning, but the chances are ' that
she will be a few hours late and will not
dock until to-day. Captain Hall will be
able to work his men in discharging and
has no fear of a walkout when the vessel
ties up.
"Water Front Notes.
The wrecking schooner Bessie K re
turned from Half moon Bay yesterday
with the windlass and thirty fathoms of
chain from the City of Florence.
The British steamer Monmouthshire
that was here last year in the California
and Oriental Steajnship line has been sold
to the Quehec Steamship Company and
will run in the Windward Island trade.
The army transport tug Slocum got
back to port yesterday. At Santa Bar
bara she learned that the Lennox had
reached San Francisco, so Captain Mar
shall hurried back here.
O. Wallace, a negro woman, drank car
bolic acid in a water front resort yester
day and was hurried to the Harbor Hos
pital and pumped out. She was not badly
damaged.

The Brabloch will load \ wheat at Portland
for Europe, 37b 6d; the British steamer Cym
beline, wheat here for Europe or- Mediter
ranean ports, 40s; the MacMahon, wheat here
for Europe, 38s; the Eric, lumber on Puget

NOTEâ The high and low . waters occur at

The Haddon Hall had a fair run from
Liverpool here. The flrst part of the voy
age was marked by ligHt winds and
calms. On May 21 she was caught In a
very heavy east-northeast gale. It was
accompanied by a high, confused sea and
the ship was swept continuously. Four
bulwark stanchions were broken and sev
eral chain plates carried away. Consid
erable damage was done about the deck
and everything movable was washed
overboard. The run from 50 to 50 was
made in twenty days and then fair sail
ing weather was encountered. Off shore
a dense fog was run into, which delayed
the ship several days.
Away back in 1880 Captain J. B. Pritch
ard, then second mate of a wooden ship,
went home for a visit. His younger
brother was also home for a run and the
two of them "swapped lies" for over a.
week. Then they separated, and although'
each has circumnavigated the globe every
year since they never chanced to meet
again until yesterday. In 1889 Captain J.
B. Pritchard was appointed master of the
Glenesslin,- while at the same time his
brother was made chief officer of the
Haddon Hall. In 1894 Captain O. Pritch
ard received his command and has sailed
the Haddon Hall ever since. The two
vessels are chartered to load wheat for
Europe and will probably get away from
here about the same time.
Glenesslin was tied up. Seventeen of her
"lay days" have gone, but Captain Pritch
ard did not mind that, as every- day
brought his brother nearer to San Fran
cisco. After a long run the Haddon Hall
finally made port and the brothers met
again after a separation of twenty-one
years.

Admiral Dewey was in conference to
day with Acting Secretary Hackett rel
ative to the retired rear admiral to be
designated for service on the court of
inquiry. The department is now in tel
egraphic communication with an officer
who' may be given orders.
CALL. BUREAU.- 1406 ! G STREET. N.
TV.. WASHINGTON. Aug. 5.â That a full
and complete Investigation of the Samtf
son-Schley controversy will be made by
the Dewey ceurt of inquiry is shown by
the action taken by Acting Secretary
Hackett in ordering witnesses from for
eign stations to. Washington. Before his
departure from this city Judge Advocate
General Lemly submitted to .Hackett a
list of witnesses who could offer import
ant testimony and in the list was Com
mander Schroeder, - at present serving as
Governor of Guam and/during the war
with Spain executive officer of the battle-
Ehip Massachusetts. Hackett cabled to
Rear Admiral Rogers, directing him to
send the Yorktown to Guam to convoy
Commander Schroeder to the United
States.
Other officers who will be brought to
Washington include Lieutenant Com
mander Giles B. Harbor, naval attache
in Paris and St. Petersburg, who was
executive officer of the battleship Texas;
Lieutenant Commander Alexander Scharp.
who commanded the gunboat Vixen and
who is now executive officer of the Hart
ford; Lieutenant Commanders S. P. Com
ley. attached to the Alliance; L. C Hell
ner, on duty at the Xew York yard; Wil
liam H. Scheutze and W. H. Allen, at
tached to the bureau of equipment : A.
C. Hodgson, stationed at Newport; JE. T.
Capehart, on duty on the New York, ar.d
H. P. Huse, commanding the Villalobos,
who were navigators of the fleet.
Commander Schroeder will be able to
reinforce the testimony of Rear Admiral
Higgineon, who commanded the Massa
chusetts. As executive officer he was ac
quainted with the routine of the ship and
great stress is placed upon his evidence.
Whether he will return to Guam is not
certain. There have been complaints
made against his administration in conse
quence of the scandal in the marine de
tachment stationed at Agana. It is ,
thought here that Commander Swift of
the -Yorktown will temporarily relieve
Commander Schroeder as Governor.

In all of his efforts to have the "feeders"
of the Southern Pacific added to the main
system Â¦ Toland received but little encour
agement from the other members of the
board. He said that the law prescribed
that such roads should be considered with
the main system. The other members con
tended that that was a question of law â
whether the board had jurisdiction over
the "feeders"â and that the question could
be easily settled by an opinion from the
Attorney General in the matter. Toland
reminded his colleagues that they had had
ample opportunity to obtain an opinion
but had not seen fit to do so. The board
will take up the matter of county assess
ments next Tuesday.
Chairman Alex Brown agreed with him
in his contention that the representation
should be made. Toland received no sec
ond to his amendment. The board, how
ever, fixed the entire mileage of the
Southern Pacific Company at 2099.23 and
the assessment at $22,600,000. Other assess
ments were fixed as follows: ' â¢
When the board took up the assessment
of the railroads this afternoon Equalizer
Toland moved as an amendment to Col
gan's motion fixing the assessment of the
Southern Pacific Company at $22,500,000 on
2089.02 miles, that the 262,842 miles of "feed
ers" operated by the company be added
to the mileage of the company's main sys
tem. He said that the "feeders" are op
erated together in one system and belong
to the' Southern Pacific, and their earn
ings are returned as part of the general
earnings of the company. He objected to
the form of Colgan's motion. He wanted
the board to flrst establish the mileage
and then fix the assessment.

STOCKTON. Aug. 5.â The Japanese
section gang, numbering eighty men,
working at Escalon on the Santa Fe road
became involved in a riot Sunday noon,
and but for the prompt action of Ira
Clark, the timekeeper, and George Dubly,
the foreman of the gang, serious trouble
would probably have resulted. Af it was,
one of the Japanese was nearly killed.
A Japanese named Kushi was accused
by one of the men with having stolen a
monkey wrench. A demand was made
that Kushi be discharged, which was re
fused. Thereupon only about seventeen
of the gang reported for work. The re
organized and made an attack on Vne
men at work, particularly Kushi. The
men attacked were put into freight cars
by Timekeener Clark. who r with the as
sistance of Dubly, kept the mob at bay,
word in the meantime having been sent
to Sheriff Sibley at Stockton.
The Sheriff, accompanied by Deputy
Smith, went to the scene and arrested
three of the ringleadersâ Morshita. Ka
tuski and Shimnski. who, with Kushi,
were brought to the County Jail. Kushi
is badly cut about the head and bears
other marks of rough handling. At one
time during the fracas about twenty of
the rioters pounced upon Kushi and would
have killed him but for the timely assist
ance of Clark, who is said to have a re
markable influence over the gang. The
arrested men will probably be charged
with assault to murder.

Canada Prepares for the
Duke and Duchess
of Cornwall.
Unique Celebration to Be
Held at Vancouver in
October.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
VANCOUVER, B. C, Aug. 5.â Great
preparations are being made throughout
Canada for the reception of the Duke and
Duchess of Cornwall and York, who will
visit the Dominion in September and Oc
tober. The Ophir, on which the royal
party is traveling around the world, will
arrive at Halifax about September 15, and
from there until they arrive in Vancouver
on October 1 there will be daily official
receptions in all the principal cities along
the route. The preparations going on now
in Vancouver for the greatest celebration
ever seen here are a fair sample of what
Is being done in all the Canadian cities,
and altogether the tour will be quite the
most aristocratic function since the days
when King Edward visited Canada more
than forty years ago. *â¢Â¦'.'*,
Besides their own suite their royal high
nesses will be welcomed at Halifax and
accompanied right across the continent by
Lord MInto, the Governor General; Lady
Mlnto, Sir Wilfrid Laurier, the Prime Min
ister; Sir Richard Cartwright, Sir Louis
Davies, the Hon. David Mills and other
members of the Federal Cabinet.
Trip to the Alaska Coast.
From Halifax the itinerary will include
St. John, N. B.; Montreal, Ottawa, To
ronto, Quebec, Winnipeg, Regina, Calgary
and Vancouver. Other Eastern cities will
be visited on the return tour, after their
royal highnesses have visited Victoria and
taken a trip up the Alaskan coast. The
Canadian Pacific has placed the steamship
Empress of India, which will be in port,
at the disposal of the party to make the
run to Victoria and later take the north
ern trip if the latter is arranged.
In order to make satisfactory arrange
ments Mayor Townley of Vancouver re
cently sent a special envoy to Ottawa,
where a statement of the exact pro
gramme to be earried out in Vancouver
was obtained. The plan is on a grand
scale and will contain features absolutely
unique in Canada.
The special train of the royal party will
arrive in Vancouver at 11:30 o'clock in the
morning, and the Duke and Duchess will
remain here until next morning. The flag
ship Warspite of the North Pacific squad
ron and several of the other warships will
be in port. A request has been forwarded
to the United States Navy Department to
allow the Oregon or one of the other of
Uncle Sam's warships on the Pacific to
be here and join in the celebration. A
naval guard of honor will be in attend
ance and the flagship band with local
bands will play "God Save the King" as
the train comes in.
Ceremony at Vancouver.
His Excellency the Governor General
will then introduce the Lieutenant Gov
ernor of the province. Sir Henry Joly, Ad
miral Beaumont and Mayor Townley to
their royal highnesses. The ladles will
present to the Duchess a bouquet of flow
ers and immediately after the civic ad
dress of welcome will be read by the
Mayor. The latter will then escort the
Duke to. his carriage. Whether the as
sembled crowd will follow or precede the
procession uptown will appear in printed
instructions later..
The party will be driven to the new
military drill hall, which will be opened
by his royal highness, and at the conclu
sion of this ceremony luncheon at the Ho
tel Vancouver will be announced by the
Mayor. . There areto be no speeches at
lunch. Afterward the Mayor is to pro
pose the health of the King and later the
health of the royal visitors, which will
then be drunk. The Duke will reply to
both at one time.
Headed by a detachment of mounted
police in uniform, the party will drive
around the park in the afternoon and
later will be conducted through the Has
tings sawmill, one of the largest on the
coast. In the evening there will be an
Illuminated procession of fishing boats on
the inlet. Including Indian war canoes,
there will probably be over 2000 boats in
the procession, so that this spectacle alone
will be a unique one. Early next morn
ing the party will leave for Victoria by
the Empress.
HOTJSE OF LORDS DECIDES
AGAINST A TBADES UNION
Dismisses an Appeal From a Judg-
ment Rendered Against a Butch
ers' Association.
LONDON, Aug.. 5.â The House of Lords
to-day rendered an important decision af
fecting trades unions. The case was
brought up on appeal from the judgment
of the Irish courts awarding a wholesale
butcher damages against the Butchers'
Assistants' Association, which brought
pressure to bear on retailers to boycott
the platintiff until he dismissed a certain
non-union employe, threatening otherwise
to call out the union employes of the
firms concerned, and finally induced the
non-unionist to leave the plaintiff. The
Lords dismissed the appeal on the ground
that the alleged acts of wrongful and
malicious conspiracy were not undertaken
to advance the interests of the workmen
themselves, but solely to injure the plain
tiff. : -
Throws Himself Into a Furnace.
SALT LAKE, Aug. 5.â A special to the
Deseret News from Provo, Utah, says
that John M. Wilson, a patient at the in
sane asylum, threw himself head first into
,the open door of a furnace to-day. He
was badly burned before being pulled
out. While bandages were being prepared
Wilson broke away and again threw him
self into the furnace, this time being so
frightfully burned that he died within a
short time.

Alcazar.
The Board of Health will endeavor to
have a law passed at the next meeting of
the Legislature compelling young men in
pood circumstances to make provision for
their Indigent parents. As things are now
any number of children shirk the burden
of their responsibilities, and the result is
that a large number of aged persons make
application to be admitred to the Citv and
County Almshouse who have children who
are -well able to take care of them. This
has resulted in such a congestion at that
Institution that the Board of Health Is
forced to think up some plan to relieve it.
It is thought that such a law would be
very effective, as few sons would be will
ing to appear, in court as the defendant
in such a case.
Children Must Care for Parents.

Discovery Made by De-
tective Armed With
Search "Warrant."
Detective Ryan succeeded yesterday In
locating a. portion of . a consignment of
carpets valued at $2000 that was stolen
from the Indianapolis Furniture Company
at 750 Mission street on March 16 of the
present year. He secured a search war
rant in Judge Cabaniss' court and when
he visited the ' place where "the stolen
property was supposed to have been
stored was rewarded by finding a portion
of it. As yet the thieves have not been
apnrehended. but warrants Â¦will probably
be"procured for their arrest to-day.
On the night of March 16 the large store
room of the furniture company was en
tered and looted. Some of the carpets
taken were 'among the most valuable In
stock and the robbers profited handsome
ly by their haul. They must have used
an express wagon, which they drove
around to the rear of- the store and loaded
with their booty. Â¦ â¢ "
The police were at once notified and De
tective Ryan took charge of the case. He
was unable for some time to get on the
track of the burglars or locate their loot.
About one week ago. however, he ob
tained a clew that a portion of the stuff
had been stored In a house in the imme
diate neighborhood, and accordingly ap
plied for the search warrant yesterday.
He found that he was on the correct trail
and discovered about $300 worth of carpet
which the furniture people identify as a
portion of that which was taken from,
their storeroom.
Meeting of A. O. IT. W. Lodges.
There was a good attendance last night
In the hall of Bernal Lodge of the Ancient
Order of United Workmen, at: Valencia
and Sixteenth streets. The occasion was
a district meeting of Bernal, Crocker, Noe
Valley, Eureka Valley, Excelsior, Golden
West and Bay View lodges. There were
many ladies present. In addition to ad
dresses by Brother York of the extension
committee and Grand .Recorder Spencer
on the work of the order, there was an
interesting programme of music, songs,
fancy dancing and recitations contributed
by J. Finney. Gertie Brown, Florence Fe
gan. Gertie Reed, Dollie Fegan and Grand
Receiver Sam Booth. Dancing followed
the programme. Brother Herzog of the
extension committee was the master of
ceremonies. % Â¦

When the telephone message was re
ceived at the Southern Station that a man
in room 30. 906 Market street, wanted to
be taken home, as he was afraid of strik
ers, the officers thought a non-union man
had been eorralod anu needed their aid.
They were considerably surprised on their
ai rival at the address given to find that
the iriEn who appealed so pitifully over
the nhene was a special officer named
Reed. They were a disgusted lot of po
licemen, but they bundled the quaking
special into the patrol wagon and took
him to his home at 327 Clementina street.
That they did not stop to tuck him safely
in bed was because of their anxiety to be
rid of their charge.
The spectacle of three of Chief Sul
livan's finest escorting a special police
man, armed with pistol and club and a
five-pointed star to his home was one of
the queer scenes to be met with last
night. That he was escorted home was
due to the fact that in telephoning to the
Southern station he did not mention- that
he was a special policeman.

HENRY ARTHUR JONES has
Â¦written a great play. Marparet
Anglin is a great actress and the
Empire Theater Company is an
unqualified success; such was
the verdict of last night's audience at the
Columbia Theater at the giving of "Mrs.
Dane's Defence."
Oh, but it is clever! The little country
village of Sunningwater, with its world
over types, Mrs. Grundy, Mrs. Bulsom-
Porter; the incarnate spirit of compro
mise, Canon Bonsey; the law legal, Sir
Daniel Carteret; the beautiful sinner who
has committed the crime of being found
out, Mrs. Dane; the youthful idealist.
Lionel Carteret; the ingenue. Janet Col
quhoun; the kindly woman of the world,
Lady Eastner, who achieves Christ-like
ness through what Canon Bonsey would
term her latidudinarian views, and the
old, old rest of them.
All portraits these, drawn with a few
sure, strong touches, with never a false 1
tone or accent throughout, and posed in
the "picture with an inspired sense of dra
matic light and shade. Quite devoid of
theatricalism Is "Mrs. Dane's Defence,
but withal intensely dramatic, with the
drama largely played by the audience, ex
cept in the wonderful third act. The play
wright, with a positive genius of direct
ness, plunges Immediately into the heart
of his subject, placing his audience en
rapport with the story right from the be
ginning, and from the beginning to the
endâ no! the end is anti-climaxâ but almost
to the end. holds them with caught breath
and a pull at the heart.
Mrs. Dane is a woman with a pastâ the
usual kind of pastâ beautiful, rich, young,
charming, who has come to the village of
Sunntngwater to "live it down," her own
memory of. It only, for it is known to no
one there. Being what she is, and without
visible ancestry, she arouses the jealousy
and suspicion of Sunningwater's society
leader, Mrs. Bulsom-Porter, who is every
thing that Mrs. Dane is not.
This ferociously respectable and un-

MEDICINEâ W. N., City. One who is
not a physician may engage in selling
patent medicine without having a llcenso
required of medical men to practice med
icine. In San Francisco if he sold such
medicine at a fixed place of business he
would not require a municipal license, but
as to the whole United States this depart
ment cannot inform you. If, however, in
San Francisco you should peddle the med
icine you would be liable to pay a ped
dler's license, and If you should attempt
to practice medicine without a license
from the Board of Medical Examiners
you -would be liable to prosecution.

BUT FOR A DAYâ M. B., Tomales, Ca!.
The insect that lives but a day is called
the ephemera. If Js the linnaean genus
of neuropterous insects now forming the
family or tribe ephemerlde. They have
received their name from the brief dura
tion of their existence In the perfect
state, talcing no food, merely propagat
ing their species and dying. The ephe
mera,, when it leaves its grub life in tha
water and assumes a higher form, and
aerial existence, dies the very* day it was
born into its new life.

PORCUPINE-S. P. R., West Oakland.
Cal. The porcupine, or hedgehog. Is a
genus of mammalia of the order rodentia
and family hystricidae. This family is re
markably characterized by an armature
of spines, which as to structure are mere
ly'thick and strong hairs. Its general as
pect is piglike and heavy. It has a grunt-
Ing voice, but has not. as you suppose,
wings. The German for porcupine is sta
cfcelschwein (thorn pig), in Italian it is
porco spinoso (pig with spines). In Latlri
porcus spinosus (thorny pig).

A CHANCE TO SMILE.
PEDROâ Subscriber, Newman, Cal. "A
and B are playing pedro. Each wants one
point to go out. A bid3 two. makes
high, jack and" game and B makes low;
which wins?" ask3 this correspondent. A
wins. He bid two, made all he bid and
more, too, and. having high, counts first,
for the count is high, low, jack, game and
pedro.

wPERSONAL MENTION.
IT was announced in the Berlin cables several weeks ago that Emperor "Will
iam has commenced to let his beard grow and that he now, more than ever,
bore a striking resemblance to his father. The Call Is able to present a pic
ture of the German ruler, showing how he looks Â¦with hla latest hirsute adorn
ment. The Emperor, in May of last year, began to modify somewhat the fierce
upward twist of his mustache, which he reduced to an angle of forty-five degrees.
About the same time he dismissed the imperial barber, Haby, who is said to have
become impudent and to have presumed to trade on his supposed' friendly rela
tions with the Emperor. It was also said then that the Emperor was about to
let his beard grow, and German barbers were in despair, for the fashion of wear

extent, and thus the movement toward reform has
been actually started. Doubtless the adoption of 7 a
system of taxation similar, to that in France would
prove advantageous in limiting the evil as well as in
bringing in a considerable revenue to the community.
At any rate the persistency with which the Eastern
press is urging. the reform gives assurance that a
strong public sentiment will soon^be developed in
favor of it. When that sentiment is aroused a means
will-be found to rid the country and the cities of a
nuisance" which has been already too long tolerated.
It may be^.long time before American landscapes
and American cities are redeemed, from- the bill
posting and fence advertising nuisance, but sooner or
later the reform will be accomplished. Steps have
been already taken to restrict the nuisance to some
From Philadelphia a simila- complaint comes. The
Times says any American who returns home from a
prolonged sojourn abroad will-be struck by the great
number of advertising signS that desecrate every
available space, and by_ way of .abating the nuisance
it advocates the adoption in. Pennsylvania . of the
French system of taxing such forms of advertising.
"It-is a valuable privilege," says the Times, "in the
nature of a franchise like that which is given out. to
authorize the use of the streets by a railroad company
and other permits that bring benefits to private indi
viduals. It is worth. money to an advertiser to put up
a notice of his wares in a public place where thou
sands of people may see<it. Furthermore, it is in the
interest of dignity, art and an undefiled landscape that
these pasters and painters should not go about over
the face of the earth with a free hand."
The effect of unlimited bill-posting and fence adver
tising has been a renewal of the agitation in the East
for the enactment of laws to prevent any further per
petration of the nuisance. The New York Sun in
commenting upon the evil in and around the city says
illuminated signs have been placed along the Pali
sades, that the Brooklyn Bridge seems to exist only
for the sake of serving as. a framework on, which to
hang the names of all sorts of articles of commerce,
and that electric light advertising has reached the
limit or^ Broadway. It adds: "It is reasonable to
resent anything which takes from the beauty of a
piece of scenery, or anything which takes from the
dignity of a city's principal thoroughfare. If we have
the right to protection from discomforting noise, why
should we not have some protection from what jars
us through the sense of sight?"
WHEN the French were preparing for "the
Paris Exposition of 1900 they sought to add
to the attractiveness not only of their," capi
tal'but of their whole country by strictly forbidding
anything in the way of bill-posting mrisances^either
around the exposition, grounds or about the stations
of the railways by^which visitors reached the city.' In
that way they furnished the civilized world with an
example of reform that was widely commented upon
and did much to encourage the figKt against such
nuisances elsewhere. In Buffalo something like an
opposite course appears to have been pursued, and
as a result the poster advertising around Niagara
Falls is said to have been carried to such an extent
as to seriously detract from the enjoyment of the
visitors. *' *

some one has died of it in that locality.

floating around inj the East for a long time. Fortu

hood it has a hard task. In'the end truth lives and

A LONG-LIVED FALSEHOOD.
In ordering Air. Kennan out of Russia the Czar
was probably unaware that he had given that distin
guished lecturer a big*free advertisement for his next
course of lectures on the barbarity of. the Russian
Government. s "..'.-
/^> IXCE the renewal of the French vineyards by
stocks from California that are ; resistant of
phylloxera, the crop has so enormously in
creased that prices have fallen below any possible
profit. The vineyardists complain, however, that the
increased yield is accompanied by a decreased mar
ket, and that California,- taking advantage of the
shortage in past years caused by the destruction of
the French vineyards, has occupied their market.
We have not at hand statistics to prove or disprove
this contention, but we assume that the Frenchmen
know what they are talking about. It must mean that
California has taken possession of the wine and
brandy market in this country and Great Britain, and,
perhaps, to an extent, in Central and South
America and Russia. We are aware that California
vintners have recently complained of the bad state of
their market, but they attribute it to the "brickyard
vineyards" of the Lake Erie region, Missouri and
Central New York. But this complaint, if justifiable,
only shows -that American wine-drinkers are using
the American product in preference to the foreign,
and are tricked into patronizing the blended and re
inforced artificial wines of the East for lack of
knowledge of what wine really is.
The only genuine wine grapes grown in this coun
try are grown in California. A wine grape must have
in the fruit all of the elements required in a finished
Wine, the sugar, the acid and, if red wine, the color
and grape tannin. Nothing need be added to give
the v.ine all the qualities required by the user. The
Eastern grapes have neither the sugar nor the color
-required. They are tteated with syrup and get their
color for aniline dyes. As they lack sugar they fall
below the antiseptic percentage of alcohol, which is
supplied by corn high wines. If short in tannin they
are filled with oak tannin. The grapes supply some
acid and the juice necessary to fermentation, and that
fs about all..
The California vintners can help their domestic
trade by publishing widely a plain statement of facts
about wine, what it is, how made, and the necessary
elements for its excellence, which must exist in the
grape and cannot be supplied I from other sources
without injuring the wholesomeness of the product.
The Hammondsport (N. Y.) wines, grown on
Keuka Lake, and the Ohio wines from KeMy's Island,
had quite a hold in the Eastern market before Cali
fornia began exporting. The great headway made by
our wines was against their competition, at the out
set, and it would seem that there is nothing in their
competition now that should seriously bar the
progress of our vintage.
Having secured a market by the superior quality
of our vintage, it can be held only by rightly observ
ing the principles of commercial honor, and sending
out only sound, aged and finished wines. If our
vintners attempt to meet the competition of sophis
ticated wines by sophisticating, they will make a mis
take that means their final ruin. . â¢-.Â¦
â¢ A movement has been started in the East to. present
a loving cup to Admiral .Cervera in recognition of his
kindness to Lieutenant Hobson when that dashing
young officer was taken prisoner at Santiago, and it
is to be hoped the, scheme will go through.' Amid the
rivalries and bitternesses of commerce it is pleasant
to find the fighters doing the fraternal act.
' The people of Europe never realized the largeness
of the United States until recent events directed their
attention to the fact that it is big enough. to hold
Rockefeller and' Morgan. at the same time.
THE FRENCH VINE-GROWERS.
We agree with Rosebery and Cambon, and defend
the American teacher against Professor Russell's
harmful ascription and the brutal phraseology in
which it is expressed.
The French Embassador, Jules Cambon, in a re
'cent speech at Chicago said: "America's magnificent
public school system and its host of well equipped and
richly endowed colleges and seminaries are the best
guarantees the nation possesses, or can possess, of 'its**'
future peace and stability. More than its inexhaust
ible mines of gold and iron, more than its vast and
ever-increasing number of -manufactories and railway
systems, its agricultural resources and other wealth
producing agencies, do these public schools and other
institutions of learning stand for the things that prom
ise strength, perpetuity and tranquillity to the Ameri
can republic. - The teacher is, after all, the greatest
of peacemakers. Breadth of view, refined sensibili
ties, enlarged sympathy, lofty and ennobling senti
ment, a higher range of thought and feelingâ all these
and other things developed by a true/ educational
process count against a resort to the cruelties and
barbarities of war." Â¦:
A vast majority of the millions of Americans have
come to manhood and womanhood through the pub
lic schools. If their training have been by teachers
narrow, bigoted and incompetent, then the national
character has been narrowed and bigotized. Instead
of a broad and strong people, as the world believes
us to be, we are a narrow and weak people. Our
rivals in the industrial^ world, who have critically ex
amined us, in a search for the cause of our strength
and industrial supremacy, have frankly; declared that
.it is in the public school training of our people, lead
ing to their high level of intelligence, their faultless
initiative, their individual independence. It is con
ceded that we make mistakes, but .they are the mis
takes of the strong and broad, not of the v narrow and
weak. Lord Rosebery has said that the salvation of
industrial England must be sought in the introduc
tion there of our American public school system.
Other English thinkers have taken the same posi
tion, and their attitude is so significant that it is be
lieved it foreshadows the next issue in English poli
tics. Can it be possible that all other observers, at
home and abroad, are wrong and this Columbian
pundit is right?
The trouble with Professors Russell and Wendell
seems to be a fondness for calling names and making
snoots at those who in good faith have sought their
classes to be instructed. All men know that it is
an infirmity of narrow and bigoted men to think
others to be as they are.
In such a situation the teachers and the public
have a right to know what painful experiences in his
career led Professor Russell to his conclusion. '
Professor Russell says that his damnifying conclu
sion is "the product of long thought and experience."
But Horace Mann had long thought and experience
and reached no such conclusion. Other educators,
whose eminence made them known to the public,
reached the opposite conclusion.
The qualities of narrowness, bigotry and incom
petency must be shown in methods, practices and
habits, in deed and in action. What acts of teachers,
as a class, -betray these qualities?
What he has said. and classically calls "a shake up"
does no good at all. On the contrary, it does harm.
It plants the seeds of distrust and suspicion in the
minds of the millions of parents whose children are
in the schools. It makes every. teacher's task harder
and more difficulty It makes the taxpayers less pa
tient in supporting the public schools. It hardens the
hoof of tyranny on the part of shallow school boards,
entrusted with brief authority, and is the cause of
evil, and evil only.
Professor Russell states only a conclusion, and that
in language that is offensive. He is unknown to the
general public of this State, the West and the Union.
A man must have established reputation for wisdom,
for good judgment, in order -to secure acceptance of
his conclusions without premise or argument.
We submit that his method of shaking them up
has in it no leading to reform, if reform be necessary.
The indictment of teachers, as a class, as incompetent
does no good. The professor's statement, if it have
any merit, must be based upon observed facts. In
our judgment a statement of those facts, without other
characterization, would be useful to teachers as a
class. Such a statement of facts would be his pre
mise and could be examined and validated or inval
idated, and his^ conclusion could be justified or over
thrown. The inquiry should be'What do teachers do,
and how, that exhibits their narrowness, .bigotry and
incompetency? . .
WE again hail Columbia and its Doctor of
Pedagogics, Professor James. Earl Russell.
Since his public declaration, to a class of
teachers in' the- Berkeley summer school, that their
profession is narrow and bigoted, he appears in an
interview in which he" adheres manfully to his state
ment and adds to it by saying: "I mean to say that
the teachers of the United States, as a class, are nar
row, bigoted and incompetent. .The solution of the
problem lies in the teachers themselves, and the first
step is to shake them' up and tell them frankly their
faults."

Yukon River and Its tributaries have be
come so denuded of timber that the ques
tion of obtaining fuel for Dawson and
other mining camps is becoming a se
ilous one. The transportation companies
are commencing to experiment with oil,
realizing that the small timber growing
along the Yukon cannot furnish an abun
. dant supply very much longer.
Heretofore almost any one could em
bark in the logging- business. It being
only necessary to fall trees, roll them
into the water and float them to mar
ket. At present only loggers of experi
ence can succeed, as but few logs can be
secured less than a. mile from the river's
edge. The Governor of Yukon Territory
has sent timber Inspectors to the head
waters of the Klondike end Stewart riv
ers to report as to the amount of tim
ber available in those sections. . â¢

NEW YORK. Aug. 5.â It was said to
day by iron and steel merchants here that
there was no great change in the trade
on account of the steel strike. The prices
were somewhat higher and it was becom
ing difficult to get steel for quick export
delivery. They are now accepting foreign
orders subject to such delaysas the strike
may cause. However, there has been no
marked falling off in exports, but a de
crease in the volume of foreign business
Is expected to show Itself soon. Some tin
plate was coming to the United States
from Wales, it was said, but this was not
due to the strike, nor would a long strike
in this country result In large importa
tions from England, because there is sup
posed to be no more than enough In Wales
to supply the English market.
rienced in Getting Goods for
Quick Export Delivery'.

TAMPA, Fla., Aug. 5.â The situation in
the cigar-makers' strike remains critical.
The positions of the unions and the man
ufacturers remain good, but It is feared
that the public feeling engendered against
the strikers will cause an outbreak sooner
or later. Boycotts were to-day placed on
many . business concerns because they
were reported to be friendly to certain
people who are said to oppose La Re
sistencia Union. Others who are friendly,
but who are not engaged in business, were
followed through the streets on their
every appearance by a taunting and Jeer
ing crowd. In several instances collisions
were narrowly averted.

He had been despondent for. several days
and drank prussic acid.

The condition at McKeesport at 10 p. m.
is rt-ported as feverish. Many strikers are
on the streets around the National Tube
Works. The men announce they are only
waiting for orders from headquarters be
fore going out. Organizer Flynn of the
Federation of Labor was here to-day
looking over the situation and conferring
with the local leaders. Persistent rumors
Vice President John Chappelle of New
castle was here to-day between trains and said
that all the steel mills belonging to the United
States Steel Corporation would be called out
this week- Further than that he refused to
talk.
Four new men were brought Into the mill
from Scottdale to-day, and four of the strik
ers deserted the union and went back to work.
Five mills were working to-day, and four are
working to-night, the largest number run any
day since the strike began.
The last four of the striking steel workers
who were arrested Saturday, charged with riot,
were released from Jail late this afternoon up
on ball. There are warrants yet in the hands
of the police for seventeen more of the strik
ers. The officers, for some reason, seem to be
afraid to serve the warrants. Henry B.
Henderson,- a potter for whom the police have
a warrant, haa been trying to get himself in
th^ way of the officers all day to have them
arrest him, but they will not do It for the
reason that Henderson .Is backed by the Na
tional Brotherhood of Operative Potters, and
the officers fear the vengeance of the potters,
once they are aroused, as they would be If one
of their number were put under arrest. Sec
retary Duffy of the potters, when seen to-day,
denied that the brotherhood had any Intention
of calling its members out In sympathy with
the steel workers, though he said the brother
hood would extend them both : financial and
moral aid. .--.
At 4 o'clock this afternoon the Hyde Park

Â«rlft and Apollo. The train stopped at tho

Leechburg sauntered down toward the mill
passed the guards and walked Into the mills,
where to-night they are at work. This makes
sixty-one men now employed at the works.
The mill Is running- to-night. / The Amalga
mated men. however, are on the ground, mak
ing desperate efforts to keep all union men
out. They make the bold assertion that In two
days after President Shaffer issues his general
strike order not a wheel will be turning in
Vandercrlft, Apollo or Hyde Park.

An Appeal to Compatriots.
Official announcement of the revolution
is made by General Castro in a proclama
tion issued on July 26, in which he says:
"The sacred territory of the fatherland
has been invaded by an army of Colom
bians commanded by .a traitor, Carlos
Rangel Garbiras, and they have entered
by Urena and San Antonio. In announc
ing this uprising, which affects the honor
and peace of the republic. I fulfill my duty
as the man primarily responsible for the
fate of Venezuela, and inform you that
all necessary measures have been already
taken to repel this grave attempt. In
order, therefore, to facilitate the exercise
of my discretional authority as command
er in chief of the Venezuelan army, and
at the same time to enable me to fulfill
my duties as President of the republic,
the national executive has made use of
the power conferred on it by the national
constitution and has suspended those per
sonal guarantees the exercise ' of which
may be incompatible with the defense of
public order. â¢
Castro's False Proclamation.
The Government Is trying to make the
public believe that Â¦ the movement is an
invasion of Venezuela by Colombia. This
is not the case, but if it were it would be
regarded by the people as a just recom
pense of General Castro's hostile policy
against Colombia, especially when it is
remembered that only a few weeks ago
General Uribe-Uribe, chief of the Colom
bian revolution, was transported from
Curacao to Maracaibo on the Venezuelan
warship Zumbador. This fact alone will
throw a deal of light on the condition of
affairs.
The Government has' sent 10,000 troops
against General Garbiras. who is invoking
the name of "El Mocho," the famous Gen
eral Hernandezâ that is to say, this insur
rection Is a revival of nationalism.
Revival of Nationalism.
At the news of this new Insurrection,
which had been foreseen owing to Presi
dent Castro's arbitrary manner of gov
erning the whole country is rejoicing,
and, if faUh is to be placed In commercial
reports, Is on the point of rising. Â¦
Dr. Garbiras is a physician, well known
and highly respected. He has been mak
ing preparations for this insurrection for
more than three months on the frontier of
Colombia, and on the night of July 25 5000
men occupied a position between San An
tonio and Urena, towns on the frontier of
Colombia. His men are all well armed.
