Special Dispatch. to The Call.
PITTSBURG, Aug. 5.â -Just a faint
glimmer of hope that the great steel
workers' strike would be settled was em
bodied In a statement given out by Pres
ident Shaffer of the "Amalgamated Asso
ciation to-night. When asked if he would
pursue the same policy In ordering a
strike In the mills of the Federal Steel,
the National Steel 'and the National Tube
Companies as he did in calling out the
men In the mills' of the "American Tin
Plate Company he replied: "If It had not
been for this determination on my part,
the general strike would bave.been or
dered on Saturday night." ' .:â¢
Before calling out the tin workers, and
after falling to get any satisfaction from
the officials of the American Sheet Tin
Company â¢ and the American Steel Hoop
Company, President Shaffer sent a tele
gram to Vice President Warner Arms of
the American Tin Plate Company, notify
ing him that under article 19, section 35,
of the constitution of the Amalgamated
Association, he would be obliged to call
out the tin workers in all of the mills
owned by the United States Steel Corpor
ation unless the difficulty was settled
within a period of ten days. As a resuit
of this notice Arms succeeded in getting
together another conference, and a vain
attempt was made to settle the dispute
and prevent a strike which would involve
the tin mills. That conference was the
one that broke up in the Hotol Lincoln
three weeks ago last Saturday.
In order to be equally fair to the other
constituent companies of the United
States Steel Corporation and -accord them
the same treatment President Shaffer has
sent a similar notice to the officers of the
Federal Steel Company, the National
Steel Company and the National Tube
Company, giving them the same time in
which to make any effort they may desire
to bring about a settlement, or he will
put In force this same clause in the Amal
gamated constitution. It is presumed
that the delay in issuing the general order
will be at least until the end of the pres
ent week. In the meantime the men in
the mills of these three companies will
be prepared to come out when the strike
order is issued.
The possibility of a settlement of the
strike Is based on the bare hope that the
officials of the three additional companies
will bring to bear sufficient influence to
urge an adjustment of the difficulty before
the strike order is issued. President Shaf
fer did not express any hope that this
would be done, nor did he even discuss
the matter, but the Inference was quickly
seen that there was such a probability in
sight. It is a faint one, though, and but
little interest was taken In it in the gen
eral offices of the association to-day.
Regarding the publication of charges
that the Amalgamated Association would
be violating contracts with the constituent
companies of the trust, with which they
had signed scales for the present year,
President Shaffer said that all contracts
and scales are signed at all times sub
ject to the provisions of the constitution
of the Amalgamated Association. This
constitution was in the hands of all the
officials of all the companies affected or
likely to be affected and they are fully
aware of the provisions that it contains
for this very difficulty.
Just a Faint Hope That the Difficulty
Will Be Adjusted.

"Ever' since my little girl was born

He was jealous and was always threaten

he place our little Mary where I could
have access to her. He said he knew he
could not have her by law but nothing
could prevent his stealing the child. Later
he refused to separate and told me to go
down to tny mother's, a distance of two
miles.
"I picked up the baby and took Mary by
the hand and started down the road to
my mother's. Then he pulled his gun and
made me come back- I returned and
Ftoo-l by him in the door. He said: 'For
two cents I would cut your throat and the
baby's.' He had been abusing the baby
all the morning, choking it until it grew
black in the face. Every time he looked
at the baby he would run to him and slap
him In the face. He threatened to kill me
and the children and then go down to
ray mother's and kill father, mother and
my sisters and brothers and any one else
who might come in his way. and then take
his own life. I was outdoors when he
called me in. He had a 22-caliber rifle in
his hands. He made me sit down beside
.him en the bed. Being quiet a moment or
two, he looked around and then shot at
a fowl in the yard.

By the Red L> liner Philadelphia, which
arrived here to-day from Venezuela, Agent
Guerrera received a budget of information
in the form of reports, cablegrams and
letters from th_ scene of hostilities. These
stated that General Benjamin Herrera,
who is attacking from the Pacific side, is
preparing in the department of Cauca;
General Argila, who is advancing from
the Atlantic, is in the department of San
tander and is in possession of Ocana City;
Generals A. Castillo and Clodmlro Castillo
have effected a junction of their forces
and are at El Valle, in the department of
Magdalena, and General Marin has. fought
an important battle at Jirardot, in the de
partment of Cundinamarca, which is in
the interior of the country, about 100 miles
from Bogota, the capital of Colombia. Im
portant and successful engagements have
been fought by General Herrera at Tuinco
and Barbacos,. and other Insurgent vic
tories, are reported from the southeastern
portion of the department of Cauca. A
great revolutionary army is said to be op
erating on the Pacific side of Colombia.
Information which came to Agent Guer
rera via . Mara caibo states 'that Senor
Valencia is on his way to Cucuta to take
charge of the Government forces on the
â¢frontier, and that at Bogota the mer
chants, not wishing to handle any more
paper money, have closed their stores,
and that business throughout the country
is at a standstill.

betrayed a yielding tendency from the opening

ran from two to six point? for the Ftocks most

of all principal grroupg. Occasional rallies
were unavailing In checking the steady stream

utilized everj' temporary recovery to put out
additional short lines of stocks. Western and
Pacific stocks bore the brunt of the selling In
the railroad quarter, while United States Steel
and Surar were In the van in the downward
movement of the specialties. The failure to
effect a Feitlement of tjie steel strike on Satur
day prepared operators for a resumption of
liquidation in the steel issues at the opening of
business to-day. First sales of the common
Â¦u-<?rp 15,000 shares at from 41 down to 40%,
Â¦which about measured the low point until well
In the sfternoon. when it sagged to 40. Inter
mediate transactions were on a narrow frac
tional range, but the fact that the stock was
â¢well backed ud arcund 40 did not exert any
strengthening effect on the general market. The
closing price was the lowest of the day and
indlca.ted a net loss of 3% points, as compared
with Saturday's close. The preferred stock
Euffered an extreme loss of ZVÂ» and ended three
points off. The street was inclined to view the
failure to reach an agreement as foreshadow
ing a highly sensitive market for the stock for
awhile. Other metal properties scored marked
sympathetic losses, Tennessee Coal dipping 3%
and American Car and Foundry preferred four
points. Selling by the West continued in eplte
of rezortM of rains in various sections of the
corn belt and the publication of a large number
of very satisfactory increases in railroad earn
ings for the fourth week of July. Sugar wag a
prominent feature all day and dropped B3i to
139% on sales by the professionals who argued
that the forthcoming opposition would make
large Inroads on its earning capacity. But
little news was circulated to account for the
tcute weakness of many 'stocks and thr selling
,n-as attributed in part to liquidation by holders
Â¦who have become tired out by the recent steady

A dispatch from Chicago to Bolton. de Ruyter
& Co. says that the twenty-two mills In Min
neapolis are working day and night to catch
up on orders for Flour. The entire output for
two months has been sold, and the weekly
grind now averages 330,000 barrels. It Bcenis>
that the domestic trade has been holding off
for a decline, and is now buying. European
holdings have also been large and when these
are worked off a big business Is expected from
that quarter. Millers are generally looking for
higher prices for Flour.

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
CHICAGO, Aug. 5.â September wheat opened
unchanged to %c higher .at 69%@69%c under
buying orders and an advance at Paris. Trade
was very dull for a time, however, and an
easier tone in corn found Its reflection In
wheat, which worked off to 6S%@63%c during
the first half-hour. Seaboard clearances were
again reported as heavy, while offerings from
first hands In the West were said to be light.
While gloomy tidings of the condition of the
corn crop had no marked effect In the pit
where maize for futuro delivery Is handled. It
was accepted by wheat traders as emphasizing
the bullishness of the wheat position. A fair
sized short Interest was driven to cover, Sep
tember advancing to 70%c. On profit-taking
the market fell back -slightly, but held strong
September closing l%i over Saturday at 70%c.
The corn market opened steady, but ruled
easy for a time thereafter because of general
showers West, as well as favorable tempera
tures. Crop reports came in with the usual
tone of despondency uninterrupted. Damage
is now claimed In Illinois and Iowa. The
market rallied on these reports, the close be
ing %c over Saturday at 57%c for September.
Trade In oats was moderately active, but
was mostly done by pit professionals. Sep
tember closed a shade down at 35c. â¢
Provisions were dull, only a small business
of a purely professional character being done.
September pork closed 10c lower and lard and
ribs each 2%c depressed.
The leading futures ranged as follows:
Chicago Grain Market.
Fruits of the new crop continue very strong,
with continued large purchases in the country.

LOCAL MARKETS.
Mail advices from New York say: "Con
sumptive trade in dried fruits was of fair pro
portions and the market generally was steady
and unchanged. Prunes were firm on the spot
et about Uc higher prices than were quoted
a week ago, while the situation on the coast
vas said to be stronger, the Cured Fruit As
sociation having withdrawn quotations. The
large sizes of prunes were scarce on the spot
and In good request. Sizes 70-SOs and S0-90s
Â¦were little wanted, but there is quite an ac
tive trade in 90-100s at 3'i(f 3Â»ic. Three-crown
loots raisins were a little firmer on reports
that the Cured Fruit Association has disposed
cf its holdings of three-crown to the packers.
Only a small trade is doing in spot raisins.
Currants continue dun at no further change
in quotations. Evaporated apples are firm and
unchanged. New crop evaporated were easier,
buyers showing considerably less disposition
to take hold. Small fruits were quiet."

the market of Its usual cue as to the attitude
of the foreign speculators toward the develop
ments over Sunday. The large expansion in the
loan item of Saturday's bank statement was
commented on to-day but not adequately ex
plained. The Increase Is believed to be due to
various corporate financing now in progress. .
Money rates were easy all day and the sub
treasury paid out a million dollars, on gold
receipts at Pacific Coast points. As the session
drew to a close the pressure against the stock
market increased and the lowest prices of the
day were made in the final dealings. Reports
were current that United States Steel comnifn
and preferred were to be held at 40 and 90 re
epectiully,- but the preferred nevertheless broke
to 89%. The Immense dealings in the stocks were
made within a range of a point, comparing the
opening and closing prices of the day. Rail
road stocks covered a range of two points.
Railroad bonds were fairly active but weak
in sympathy with stocks. Total sales, par
value, $2,250,000.
United States bonds were all unchanged on
the last call.

DECIDUOUS FRUITS.
Grapes are in better assortment, but supplies
are not over large and prices show little change.
Peaches are quoted weaker for clings in bulk,
the other descriptions remaining about as be
fore. Bartlett Pears in bulk are also slightly
lower. Plums and . Prunes continue very firm
at the high prices, with a brisk demand which
consumes all arrivals. Figs are still very
scarce. Berries are lower.
Melons are In larger supply and Nutmegs are
drugging the 'market. Prices are lower all
around. Cantaloupes are also In liberal supply.
Citrus fruits rule very firm under light sup
plies, as wlll.be seen' by the quotations.
Deciduous and Citrus Fruits.

SUMMARY OF THE MARKETS.
Eggs continue to advance under diminishing
receipts. , Dealers are helping the advance
along, for it will enable them to work off their
Eastern and store goods. At the same "time
there is no scarcity of stock.
Cheese rules firm at higher prices.'
. Butter Is also quoted firm, though there Is
rather more oh the market. Fine creameries,
however, bring the top quotations without diffi
culty., Â¦

NEW YORK, Aug.: 5.â The London metal
markets were | all' closed to-day . In observance
of a bank holiday. This fact caused general
dullness to prevail throughout the day in local
circles. Tin eased off about 10 points on bid
prices under a sheer lack of support, closing
very dull at $27 45@27 75. The rest of the list
was without change and at largely nominal
prices in the absence of business. Lead dull
at $4 37%. Spelter dull at $3 S0&3 95. Copper
nominal; Lake Superior. at $16 50<g:17. and cast
ing and electrolytic at $16 37%@16 62%. Iron was

LOS ANGELES, Aug. 5.â Z. Williams,
who was convicted of assaulting a child
named : Norma .Tomlinson and sentenced
to eighteen years'^ imprisonment, has been
set free. The Supreme Court reversed the
decision of the Superior "Court and or
dered 'a new trial. This morning Assist
ant District Attorney Jones announced
that the complaining witness had re
moved to St. Louis and refused to return
to Los Angeles to prosecute the case. It
was therefore ordered that Williams be
released. The principal ground for the
Supreme Court's action was the fact that
notwithstanding much testimony concern-
Ing the affair the people failed to prove
the particular, day and hour the alleged
crime was committed.

The original tunnel road scheme was
started some* twenty years ago by L. W.
Kennedy, who organized a tollroad com
pany under the name of the Oakland and
Contra Costa Road and Tunnel Company.
There was very little money put up, but
in spite of this Kennedy managed to get
the road graded to the summit of the
ridge, built several bridges' and bored a
few hundred feet of tunnel, until a sudden
flow of water washed things out. Then
the road was attached for both material
and wages and was sold out by the Sher
iff. Those who bought the rights did not
attempt to carry on the work, and it has
remained without attention untllan act
was secured from the last Legislature per
mitting counties to build tunnels on Inter
i county roads. - â¢ . . â¢
This claim of Renton, Holmes & Co. will
necessitate an examination Into the ques
tion of rights. The owners of the prop
erty through which the road was built
claim that they did not agree to surrender
any right of way to. the old company until
the road should have been completed, and
they stand ready to give a right of way
to Alameda County.
The matter has been referred to Dis
trict Attorney. Allen and the Road and
Bridge Committee to determine where the
county stands in the matter. Â¦
Meanwhile there will be a meeting be
tween County Surveyor Brown of Contra
Costa County and Acting Surveyor Prath
er of Alameda County next Tuesday to
run the level for the tunnel. Supervisor
Stowe of Contra Costa County has se
cured deeds from Mr. Buckley for the
right of way on his side of the hills and
already has men at work on the old Ken
nedy grade clearing it up and putting it
in shape. The Alameda County viewers
expect to report to the Supervisors in two
weeks. â¢ .Â¦ ' .'"'
Dear Sir: Havlne noticed recently In the
Oakland papers that steps are again to be
taken to carry out the tunnel project between
Alameda and Contra Costa counties, and that
the tunnel is to be extended and the work
carried on as originally undertaken by the Oak
land - and Contra Costa Tunnel Company, we
now beg leave to call your attention to the
fact that we acquired all the right*, privileges
and rights of way heretofore held by the Oak
land and Contra Costa Tunnel Company. We
were forced Into taking over its property rights
through furnishing materials to the original
company which they failed to pay for. We will
be pleased to dispose of our rights in this
business to your Board of Supervisors on most
reasonable terms, and we 'will be pleased to
treat with you to that end. We remain, re
epectiully yours, RENTON, HOLMES & CO.
The Board of Supervisors has struck an
obstacle to the construction of the tunnel
road between Alameda and Contra Costa
counties in the shape of a claim from
those who allege that they hold the rights
of way and privileges of the original Oak
land and Contra. Costa Road and Tunnel
Company. A letter was sent to the Board
of Supervisors this morning by Renton,
Holmes & Co. of San Francisco, In which
that firm claims it holds all the rights of
the original company and it will be
pleased to sell the same to the county of
Alameda. The letter is as follows:
To the Clerk of the Board of Supervisors
of Alameda County, Oakland, Cal.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
1118 Broadway, Aug. 5.

LONDON, Aug. 5.â Although no division
was challenged at the third reading of the
king's declaration bill this evening in the
House of Lords, it is generally believed
that no further attempt will be made to
pass it, either this session or the ne.xt.
Lord Salisbury, the Premier, referring to
the statement of Catholic peers, said the
Government now realized that the Catho
lics did not -wish the offensive words of
the original declaration to be, withdrawn
unless the Government at the same time
withdrew the declaration regarding the
security of the Protestant succession. The
Government never had . the slightest In
tention of withdrawing that. The Cath
olics regretted that they must be pre
pared to see the declaration stand in Its
present form.
KING'S DECLARATION BILL
WILL NOT BE PASSED,

PHOENIX, Ariz., Aug. 5.â James Ever

Being unfamiliar with the stream he rodq
into the quicksand Â¦ and the horse sank.

a stage coach, hurried to the rescue. He,

lariat of a cowboy saved him, while Ever
sole sank from sight. 'Â¦...Â¦. Â¦ - .

Customs searchers, while overhauling

malan insurgents.

. NEWPORT, Aug. 5. â On board tha In

CHICAGO, Aug. 5â After a visit to
President McKInleyat Canton, Senator
Cullom says there is not likely to be any
general tariff legislation next winter, but
that some of the * pendlnff commercial
treaties ought to pass the Senate.
Asked as to the story that Senator
Hanna had gone to Canton to get aid
from the President in settling the steel
strike. Senator Cullom said: f Â¦
"I' know there is nothing in that re
port. Senator Hanna. President McKin
ley and myself talked of the strike as we
did of other matters of public interest,
but there was no suggestion from Senator
Hanna at any time that he was mixing
up in the matter at all."
Speaking of , the action likely to.be taken
by Congress at the next session, Senator
Cullom said:
"We shall have to. enact some legisla
tion regarding the* Philippines, but not
much, I think. Porto Rico is now all dis
posed of and there is nothing to do for
it. As far as Cuba is concerned, it has
accepted the American proposals, and we
have nothing more to do than to let it
alone and allow it" to Â¦work out its own
salvation. From all the information I can
get, the feeling is growing stronger in
Cuba every day in favor of final annexa
tion., to this country. â¢ It will grow
stronger and stronger as the Cubans find
their neighbors in Porto Rico enjoying the
benefits of free trade with this country,
which they are debarred from." .
"What prospect is there of tariff legis
lation' by Congress next winter?" was
asked of the Senator. â¢ . . â¢
"I do not think there will .be any, at
least In the form of a general revision of
the tariff. Of course I am not In a posi
tion to speak authoritatively for the Pres
ident,-but I feel I am representing his
ideas when I say that.
"There are, however, some things we
ought to do which will amount to a par
tial revision of the tariff. There are at
present pending before the Senate some
ten commercial treaties. Â¦â¢â¢ These treaties
are all of a reciprocity character and . if
they are ratified will amount to a change
In the tariff with the countries with which
they are made. It seems to me as if it
would be wise to ratify, at least some of
them. A feeling has grown abroad that
the: United States Is disposed to act in
what may be called a hoggish manner in
its trade relations, and if these Â¦ reciproc
ity treaties can be drawn so as to be for
the mutual advantage of both countries
entering into them, I think it would be
beneficial all around."

Bâ = â ERKELEY, Aug. 5.â Dr. Archi
bald R. Ward has arrived in this
city from Ithaca, N. Y., under
' appointment as veterinarian of
the agricultural college of the
University of California, a new position
recently created by the Board of Regents.
For ten years the faculty of the agricul
tural department has asked for_the es
tablishment of a chair of veterinary sci
ence here, but it was only at one of the
closing meetings of the Regents' last term
that the request was granted. The vet
erinarian's work will be closely associated
with that of Leroy Anderson, head of the
dairy school, buildings for which are now
in the course of construction. Â¦Â¦â¢'
Dr. Ward, although still a young man,
has had many changes of fortune. He
was at one time a steamboat captain, and
holds at present a Government license to
navigate on the Hudson River. At Cor
nell he was captain of the launch which
paced the university crew.
The college from which Dr. Ward re
ceived his D. V. M. degree is the New
York State Veterinary College, a depart
ment of Cornell. It is one of The leading
colleges of its sort in this country and is
graduating men who by their broadness
of training are more than ordinarily fitted
for their chosen work. Its two leading
professors, Dr. James Law and Dr. V. A.
Moore, .under whom Dr. Ward studied,
have world-wide reputations in the sci
ence they teach.
Dr. Ward received his early education
in the public schools of Ithaca. He en
tered Cornell and graduated with the de
gree of Bachelor of the Science of Agri
culture. Three years later, during which
time he was instructor at Cornell, he re
ceived the degree of Doctor of Veterinary
Medicine. He retired trora the faculty of
his alma mater to accept the position at
Berkeley.

Miss Hooper is the youngest daughter
of Mr. and Mrs. C. A. Hooper, her father
being the millionaire lumber merchant.
She is an accomplished musician and is a
great favorite in society on both sides of
the bay.
Two years ago Mr. Crosby was gradu
ated with the class of '99 at Harvard Uni
versity. His father is very, wealthy, and
the family Is one of the oldest in Boston.
There is a little touch of romance in the
story of the young couples' love affair.
While Miss Hooper was at finishing school
in Boston she met Mr. Crosby, and in the
course of events they, discovered that
their fathers had been school friends
many years ago. Before Miss Hooper re
turned to her home she was an engaged
girl.
Preparations for the Hooper-Crosby
wedding 1 have been going on for some
time. It will take place in the new Hooper
residence overlooking the bay at the
southerly end of Hawthorne street. The
ceremony will be performed at the fash
ionable hour of 2 o'clock. Rev Dr. Mac
kenzie of the First Presbyterian Church
of San Francisco, an old friend of the
family, will be the officiating clergyman.
The wedding will be a simple affair.
Neither bride nor groom will be attended.
With the exception of a few intimate
friends the only witnesses of the marriage
will be the relatives of the young people.
Afterward they will go on a wedding trip
that will have for its termination Boston,
where they are to live in the future.

ALAMEDA, Aug. 5.â The City Trustees
to-night adopted a series of resolutions
requesting Secretary of War Root not to
hold up the tidal canal contracts, and
ordered them telegraphed to the secretary
at once. The city of Alameda Is vitally

for the half finished work is a menace toi
the health of the city.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
111S Broadway, Aug. 5.
United States Senator George C. Per
kins and Congressman "Victor H. Metcalf
have united in demanding the completion
of the. tidal canal and the Immediate ap
proval of the contract that has been held
up In Washington. This contract was ap
proved by the United States engineers In
San Francisco,, and believing that ap
proval would certainly be indorsed at
Washington the contractors have much of
'their material on the ground
This leaves things in a very peculiar
and embarrassing condition. The South
ern Pacific Company has contracted for
all of the earth that 13 to be removed
from the tidal canal and has laid an elab
orate series of tracks to convey it from
the point of excavation to a fill that is be
ing made at West Oakland for the estab
lishment of new roundhouses. The de
mand wired to Washington to-day is as
follows:
General Georze I*. GillesDle. Chief of En
gineers, U. S. A., Washincton. D. Câ W*
earnestly urge Immediate approval of contract
for Oakland harbor tidal canal. The work
was authorized by Congress, money appropri
ated therefor, bridges have all been built, thÂ»
railroad company paid fifty thousand dollars
for its right of way, Sauaal Creek diverted,
and some of the excavatine done. Work was
commenced on Oakland harbor in 1874, and we
feel that there should be no further delay,
especially as your department promised us dur
ing the last session of Congress that the work
should be Immediately prosecuted to a final
completion. GEORGE C. PERKINS.

In accordance with the request the con-
PTegations of the First Methodist, the
First Congregational and the First Pres
byterian churches have given formal ex
pression to their support of the league
movement.
The first move on the part of the league
to give public expression to its sentiment
and purpose has been to send out to all
of the clergymen of this city a letter
which is to be presented to all of the
congregations hereabout as soon as the
communications have been received.
The letter is signed by Dr. Baker in his
capacity as president of the Northern
California branch of the league. It
reads as follows:
Oakland. Cal., Aur. S. 1901.
My Dear Brother: You are doubtless aware
ol the effort being made to abolish the bars on
our ferry boats. The liquor men are rallying
their forces and using all the influence of their
great organization to accomplish the defeat of
this effort in behalf of righteousness and order.
They have circulated a petition and secured
iTjany signatures asking that Â¦ the bars be
allowed to remain. Unless some action is taken
by the Christian people In this vicinity the
impression will prevail that the public favors
the continuance of this evil.
The friends of sobriety and righteousness
rourt not be silent on this important question,
but must boldly uphold those in authority who
are endeavoring to Introduce this much-needed
reform. To the end that the Christian people
may be afforded the opportunity to express
themselves, we request that at your regular
Rerviee to-morrow you present this matter to
your congregation and secure an expression
from them by rising vote, upholding President
Hays in his effort to overthrow this Influence
lor evil. > '

"The growers feel utterly helpless," said
Daniel 'McCarthy of San Leandro, who is
much interested in plans for enlarging the
market for Alameda County fruits and
produce. "If the goods are shipped they
are likely to come back to the shipper
only to spoil and he pays the freight. The
strike means thousands of dollars of loss
to the agricultural interests of Alameda
County."- â¢
Many of the growers are turning to the
driers for relief. A great deal ol! fruit is
being dried at Haywards and Niles in the
.hope of saving it in that way. Driers
are working full time and plants are be
ing enlarged for the emergency.
The remarkable fact is that nearly all
of the fruit growers of Alameda County
made contracts for their goods with can
ners outside of Alameda County. This
necessitates the delivery of fruit by rail,
and while they can, get the goods to some
railroad station they cannot get them de
livered to the canneries. .
This loss falls entirely upon the farm
ers, for when the canneries, particularly
those in the cannery combine, made their
contracts with the' farmers for fruit and
vegetables they inserted a strike clause
which makes the goods returnable to the
shipper In case of a strike, and the ship
per not only loses the value of his goods
but is obliged to pay the freight. Several
carloads of fruit have been returned to
shippers in Alameda County..
No class of men feel the strike more
thanr the fruit growers and farmers of
Alameda County. The great bulk of the
acreage of thlg county is set out in fruits
and vegetables for canning and preserv
ing, and when the strike came the, farmers
found that it was impossible to get their
goods to the canneries.

| BELGRADE, Servia, Aug. 5.â The first
elections under the new constitution have
resulted ; in the return of eighty-three
Ministerialists, of which number seventy
one . are Radicals. The remainder are
Liberals and Independents. Thirty-eight
second ballots are necessary. The new
election laws are regarded as very liberal.
The registration amounted to 262,000 out
of 2S5.00O eliglbles. Secrecy of- the ballot
was secured by inclosing the voting blip
in a rubber ball. Members of each party
erect their, own rubber line of .urns in the
voting booth. The voter pushes his nst
into the- funnel-shaped'.; mouth . of. esich
urn,' so the officials do not "know where
the voter -has deposited his ballot. Â¦;â¢
Secrecy of the Ballot Is Secured by
Inclosing the Voting Slip jn
a Rubber Ball. Â§

THE NEW ELECTION LAWS
LONDON, Aug. 5.â Late dispatches re
ceived from Portsmoutn regarding Itae
wreck of the torpedo-boat destroyer Viper
off Alderney Island say that it Is believed
that the well-known difficulty in reversing
turbine engines contributed to the acci
dent.
A French fishing smack, acting as a
pilot, helped the crew to reach the shore.
The. Viper Was running at twenty knots
when the fog set in. She was then eased
down to five knots and was running at
that speed when she struck. When her
cables parted her stern smashed on the
rocks. She subsequently parted amid
ships and is now in three pieces, the stern
end being inverted on the rocks. If thore
had been much sea running when the acci
dent occurred it is probable that every
body aboard of her would have perished.
As it was the crew must have perished
but for the assistance rendered.

Fishing Smack.

! At' a meetingof the Board of Election
Commissioners held yesterday, the Regis
trar was directed .to remove from .the rolls
the | names : of twenty-four men .who . are
supposed to be stuffers. All these persons
were registered from cheap lodging houses
and when cited to come before the board
and Â¦" make r. sufficient .:-- explanation they
failed to put in an appearance. Five hun
dred others who "registered" from, lodging
houses in - 1 he I Twenty-eighth, : Twenty
ninth. Forty-fourth and - Forty-fifth dis
tricts . had "moved" marked after their
names. These are also \ suspects and will
be put through a rigorous examination if
they ; are 'discovered attempting to vote
The- commissioners also passed a resolu
tion suggesting that no candidate for dele
gate to any convention should serve as an
election officer. .In many : of the districts
convention delegates have been chosen to
serve in such a capacity, v There is no law
prohibiting a"; delegate acting, as ; an elec
tion officer,' and; the resolution of 'the com
missioners is merely, condemnatory of th*
good taste of such a proceeding.

Attorneys Raker and Harris of Modoc
County, counsel for the Lookout lynching
suspects, filed a petition for a. writ of
habeas corpus in favor of their clients in
the Supreme Court yesterday. It is
claimed that the evidence presented to the
Grand Jury did not warrant the Imprison
ment of the accused, and that at any
rate they should be confined without bail.
Try to Free Lynching Suspects.
The light and water companies of the
city are - complaining at the recent order
of the Supervisors making them responsi
ble for inspectors' fees for all trenching
work they do. They claim that the city
ordinance's illegal and that they have a
right to proceed with such work under the
State law, which permits them to open up
streets upon filing a proper bond. The
ordinance has not been complied with nor
will it be until an amicable suit which has
been brought to test its legality has been
decided one way of the other.

Kelly. and.Crimmins, who are working
und^r cover and playing on the vanity of

League Club, 'is in danger of being used
as an instrument of Boss Kelly.
Workingmen, business men, merchants
and others of the Thirty-seventh District
who are sincerely devoted to the cause of
honest municipal government should in

Though Mrs. Kincaid says, she has
heard nothing of any movement to class
ify the public schools on the forty-five
pupil limit, yet the board has decided on
such a classification, though it will prob
ably be several days before active work
of reorganization will commence. Last
year the classes ran from fifty to fifty-five
and this resulted in such a congestion
that the reorganization decided upon has
been deemed a necessity.
Classification of Public Schools.
The allied bosses, with Martin Kelly in
command, intend to make a desperate
struggle to fasten boss rule on San Fran
cisco. In the Democratic camp apathy
prevails. Sam Rainey, convinced that the
Phelan-McNab combination cannot be
broken, has given orders for the Demo
cratic push to get In behind Boss Martin
Kelly. Yesterday the word was passed
along the line by Eddie Greaney telling
the Democrats who take orders to stand
in and save Kelly from defeat. This is an
old game of the bosses. It was * .often
played by Buckley to demoralize .Repub
lican forces. It is to be reintroduced by
Rainey. Citizens should -understand that
Rainey and Kelly are in close touch.

terest in the impending contest.

Republican Primary . League.
In view of this fusion of boss elements
and corrupt factions it becomes essential
that workingmen, business men, taxpay
ers and good citizens generally should re
double their efforts to save the city from
a reign of boss plunder. The bosses will
be defeated if citizens do their duty at
the primary election. Many followers of
the Democratic boss will refuse, no doubt,
to sign their names as Republican voters,
but they will be at the election booths
taking orders from Martin Kelly. It Is
a common trick of the boss rounders to
say to a voter who is known to be against
them: "This is not your precinct. You
vote at suqh'and such a number." Citi
zens who are not suspecting tricks eo
where they are directed and find that they
have been fooled. In their disgust some
decide to leave without voting. Citizens
must keep a sharp lookout for bogus i tick
ets. The bosses will freely use the motto

citizens will ,be used. The allied Â¦ bosses-

and- Herrinâ are holding back, their tick

zens whose names may be placed thereon
to decline. If leading Republicans of San
Francisco are not watchful ,Kelly-and
Kalney will use their names to boost boss
tickets -in the Twenty-eighth, Twenty
ninth, Forty-first and Forty-third Assem
bly districts. In order that no mistake
may be made voters who desire to record
an unmistakable vote against the bosses
should procure the Republican Primary
League. ticket the day before election.
Vote that tifcket and no other. No doubt
the names of good men will be placed on
other tickets, but they will be placed there
to deceive and confuse the honest voter

The jury in the case of John H. Cordes,
who was tried for the murder of John
Carlson in the Diana dance hall on Pa
cific street on April 4 last, brought in a
verdict of murder in the first degree last
night with the recommendation that the
punishment be fixed at imprisonment for
life. Cordes Is the man who killed two
men. one of them. Carlson,' and seriously
wounded a third while shooting at a
woman with whom he had consorted and
who had deserted him. .
Ccrdes Found Guiltv of Murder.
Last night Mrs. Flood surprised the po
lice by announcing that she had found the
money, and the ring under a lounge in her
rooms ' and would withdraw the charge
against Lutton.
The note showed evidence of the fact
that Lutton had known Mrs. Flood for
some time, although she declared to the
Judge, sha, had never seen him before Fri
day. In the note he wrote that he had
waited for her for half an hour and he
explained that he had been called out the
previous night on an emergency order
from the militia and had to go to the
armory and do six hours' duty. He prom
ised; to call again at 8 o'clock that even
ing. Â¦ .
Â¦ Mrs. . Flood told : a strange tale to' the
Judge.- She said that Lutton was a junk
dealer and that on Friday he called at
her house and she sold him some crock
ery ware. He picked up the diamond ring
from a table, put it on his finger and re
marked that it would look nice. When
he left she missed the ring and $35. He
called at the house next day . when shy
was out, she says, and her son saw the
diamond ring on his finger. He left a nota
for her with the boy and took $10.
Mrs. Alice Flood, 418 Ellis street, secured
a warrant from Judge Cabaniss yesterday
for the arrest of Fred Lutton on the
charg'e of grand larceny. j She accuses him
of stealing a diamond ring valued at $160.
The warrant has been placed in the hands
of Detective T. B. Gibson.
Mrs. Alice Flood Tells a Peculiar
Story About an Alleged Junk-
Dealer.
ACCUSES LTJTTON OF
STEALING HER RING
The prisoner is an ex-soldier and is 41
years of age. He says he had applied for
re-enlistment in the army. At one time
he worked for Weinhold and during his
term of employment became familiar with
the premises.
Just as Weinhold reached the rear door
a man in a soldier's uniform bolted past
him and, leaping a fence, made his escape.
Another form in citizen's attire then ap
peared in the doorway, but* Weinhold
grappled with the second intruder and
pushed him back in the room. The sa
loon man then seized a, large club and
threatened his captive with a fractured

Shortly after 8 o'clock last night .Wein

is In the rear of the saloon. Calling his
wife from upstairs, . where the family
lives, he instructed her to bar the door
that' leads into "the bar, then went around
to the rear of the room in which he had
heard the sound of footfalls and falling
bottles.
Otto "Weinhold, a saloon-keeper at Lyon
and Lombard streets, surprised two bur
glars at work in his storeroom last night.
One of the criminals, who was dressed as
a soldier, made his escape, but the saloon
man blocked the exit of the other and
held him a captive while his wife tele
phoned to police headquarters for detec
tives. The officers handcuffed the thief
and brought him to the Central Police
Station, where he gave the name of Wil
liam H. Coleman, and proved to have been
once in the employ of the man he at
tempted to rob. He was charged with
burglary.

Superintendent Leach was seen yester

secret service agent who was working in
Santa Barbara, and that the entire mat
ter of the Mint robbery was in the hands
of the secret service men. Agent Hazen
maintains his usual silence and positively
declines to discuss the robbery.
Although Dimmick is no longer a serv
ant of the Government, having been dis
missed on account of misconduct previous
to the discovery of the robberv, it is rec
ognized on all sides that he has always
been looked upon and is still regarded as
the one man who could tell where the
missing sacks of gold are located.
Dimmick's wife is now living with her
father. Judge Wright, at Santa Barbara.
Superintendent Leach stated yesterday
that he did not expect any action to be
taken in the matter of the robbery until
Director of the Mint Roberts arrived in
Washington, which will be about the 15th
inst.

In the years subsequent to the adoption
of the new State constitution it was dis
covered that many laws should be re
vised or amended. In 1895 the Governor
appointed a commission to revise the
codes, and such a commission existed
through the succeeding years up to the
session of the last Legislature, when its
recommendations were adopted.
-In anticipation of changes in the laws
the constitution had - provided for the
form in which such legislation should be
made. It seems probable, therefore, that
the manner in which revisions and
amendments were. printed for enactment
was due to an .oversight on the part of
the commission.
The titles of the proceedings heard yes
terday, and in - all of which this same
point was raised, are Gillis vs. Superior
Judge Jones of San Joaquin County, I*ev
enton vs. Judge Harrington of Modpc
County, Watt vs. Cook and Lewis vs.
Dunne of San Francisco County.
The particular cases in â¢ which manda
mus proceedings, were sought did not
bring up " the point at issue, but were
aimed at compelling the judges against
whom the. writs were directed to perform
certain duties which they maintained were
nbt necessary according. to the new codes,
but were compulsory under the old ones.
Incidentally, then, the question" as to the
constitutionality of the revisions was
raised. On the outcome of the decision
there depends the settlement of a multi
tude of cases. /
The form in which revisions and amend
ments.were presented to the last Legisla
ture was a connotation of the numeral by
Â¦which the statute is designated, followed
by the text of whatever it was desired to
repeal, add or correct. In such fragment
ary, form and apparently in violation of
the State constitution, the codes were
adopted. Unless the Supreme Court can
find a loophole of escape the original laws
will have to stand. -
No law shall be revised or amended by refer
ence to Its title, but in each case the act
revised or section amended shall be re-enacted
and published at length as revised or amended.
The question raised by the Attorney
General was whether or not section 34 of
article IV of the . State constitution had
been complied with by the Legislature.
The particular portion of the sectlfin
which refers to revisions and amendments
says: : - ! . .
The Attorney General first brought up
the matter and in every succeeding case
the same argument was made by the dif
ferent counsel. Three more hearings to
-which the same question applies are
"scheduled for â¢ to-day and the Supreme
Court must decide the issue before action
can be taken in any case.
The Supreme Court was confronted yes
terday in . five different proceedings in
mandamus with the question of .the con
stitutionality of the revised codes adopted
at' the last session of the Legislature and
which went into effect on July 1.
The secret service men here now are
uncommunicative and from all that can
be conjectured they are awaiting orders
from Burns or possibly his return from
San Francisco.
It was learned to-day that the Govern
ment officials ot the secret service have
delved into Dimmick's life thoroughly.
'liiey have also made a search for &oid
not only in Xhe house of Judge Wright,
his father-in-law, bO% it is said ii\ other
places. The officers have resented in
quiries by newspaper men and the results
of their efforts cannot be found out at
this time. That the process has been
thorough is shown by the fact that Agent'
Bums and his picked men were at work
for the space of a whole month, and not
a few days as was at first supposed.
Burns is now in San Francisco, but he
left this place so well garrisoned with his
secret forces that not a move can be made
bearing on the investigation in hand that
will not be instantly known to them.
Whether or not the authorities have any
thing against Dimmlck cannot be said,
but there is no question that he is the cen
tral tlgure in their secret inquiry here.
Judge Wright, the father-in-law of Dim
mick, is much grieved over the course
the Investigation seems to have taken and
he reiterates his assurance that Dimmlck
is perfectly Innocent. He said to-night
that his faith in his son-in-law is un
shaken. That the search of his property
was made he admits, but he says that it
came to nothing. He declines to enter
into any details about it. Judge Wright
stated that in all of Burns' dealings he
was a perfect gentleman, and, added the
Judge. "I sunpose he performed the duty
he was sent here to do."
